      \n It's hard to say. I have this feeling that so many RPG players/GMs don't actually read the instructions of the games they're playing closely, and certainly not the introductory "here's how you play this game!" stuff, that anything that's written down that isn't a mechanic or (in this case) setting information is pretty much ignored, but I equally have a hard time A) justifying this feeling with evidence, so like most of my evidence-free feelings it's probably wrong, and laying that at the feet of the text. So it would probably be best for me to edit this paragraph and delete everything but the first sentence rather than opine about a bunch of stuff I don't know about.

      The text certainly says for Storytellers to have a very clear concept for the campaign and to share it openly and aggressively with players while preparing for play. I did that and it made a BIG difference for me when I did.

      I'll probably talk a little bit more about the Chronicle section before going back and talking about the examples of play in the book because they are extremely thorough and actually stretch throughout the book.

      \n I don't mind a degree of contradiction in telling me how to fulfill a complex role according to my own preferences. Like, a good Bond game should be contradictory because the Bond franchise is big, sprawling and contradictory. Similarly with a Vampire game that is trying to support games about refugee wandering fugitives from vampire Nazis, chainsaw-wielding goth rockstars churning their way across Amurka, and people Just Trying To Become Human Again. Contradiction is fine, because those concepts are contradictory and need different things. It definitely could have been clearer, of course, but it wasn't unplayable, the contradictions weren't that significant. People read the rulebook, and didn't just throw up their hands and not play it.

      PS. Of course this is an apologia! Didn't you pay attention the last ten years, when Vampire was the Epitome Of Everything Wrong, Hurtful And Hateful In All Of Gaming Forever? When it was literally put into the same category as a concussion? If you want to hear how Vampire wrecked gaming and made people angry and sad, there's LOTS of people who will oblige you. Just one little thread of "this game was pretty great actually" won't turn aside the horrendous, godawful avalanche of shit, believe me. As soon as this thread is over, two months later some other jerk will come along saying "this game is hurtful, like that Vampire game amirite" and I'll sigh and wonder if it's worth it to pick up my musket and saber again.

      Maybe knowing which parts to read and emphasize is the real Storyteller skill here.
      \nMy experience with Vampire, which I ran straight from the book, was totally this:

      ....

      "We're gonna collaborate on the story-stuff!"

      ....

      "No, wait, I'm supposed to have a plot laid out, pretty obviously. And run it in a pretty structured, usual way. Huh, I guess there isn't any really heavy collaboration-on-story after all. It's just kind of the marketing prose. Oh, well, we're having a great fucking time anyway, with these bits here and there, and that's plenty to run a game on. So I'll do that! But I'm still gonna think about that other thing."

      ....

      And to me, the idea that people actually ran the game multiple times, but couldn't get past that thing, seems alien. Like, they wanted the collaboration-on-story-thing so bad that they just kept running into that wall, despite the fact that it wasn't very big.

      So, I'm not sure that's "skill" so much as it is letting go of that "Oh, it's gonna be this thing"

      \n In my experience, playing with various different groups over the 90s, everybody who had played rpgs other than Vampire thought they knew how to play rpgs already and even if they did read the book cover to cover, still brought stuff with them from other rpgs into Vampire. One thing I remember that's perhaps of interest, is some of the other adventures, like in the Boston book and the New Orleans book, and the Giovanni Chronicles. I learned how to run good games from Call of Cthulhu adventures, which are really good for educating you in a certain type of mystery game, if you play tons and tons of them. It's the type where you have a dastardly crime, with clues scattered all around it, and the investigators wander around until they collect enough to reach the confrontation at the end, where they all die or go insane. Vampire adventures tended to be structured as a semi-linear series of scenes, though. I never ran them as a straight railroad-through-the-scenes-in-order, but I think this presentation may have contributed to the railroad rep just as much as D:M.

      In spite of some inconsistent writing, I think you've made it clear how Mark Rein Dot Hagen intended for the game to be played, Jason. You spend a bunch of time getting on the same page beforehand, then players say what their characters do and the Storyteller says what happens. Sure.

      I have to quibble with something else, and you can always address it when you get back to that section or whatever:

      You say the powers and backgrounds are bad design in their very nature, which I find a bit odd. I think they're not really balanced all that well, but just having them is bad design? You can't solve ALL your vampiric problems by taking backgrounds, having them gives the ST something to threaten and highlight in play, and they differentiate the PCs and give them reasons to help each other, since they make characters asymmetrical in their needs. Disciplines give PCs ways to get out of trouble, sure, but again, you can't solve all your vampiric problems with them, many powers negate each other, and all of them radically differentiate characters from each other. Without them, Vampire would always be a pixelbitching urban dungeoncrawl of nightly survival. I think there's a lot of bad design INSIDE the disciplines and backgrounds, but as a whole, I don't get it. My actual play doesn't support your assessment and I can't agree with it. Am I missing something?

      \n The problem is that the Storyteller can threaten Backgrounds, but there's no mechanics for actually reducing them. As we all know, (and as we all knew even back then even if we didn't know we knew it), if there's mechanics to do a thing, and no mechanics to undo it, the thing stays done. So I could burn down your mansion and have some jackass with a cool car steal all your money, but there was no way to get Resources 5 off your sheet (that I remember or have come across yet.)

      (Actually the powers are not bad design at all...there I was trying to get across why the Disciplines actually helped players resist railroading to a degree that 1st level D&D characters just couldn't muster.)

      And what I was trying to say in my 2 Minute Hate post was that even Backgrounds aren't a problem, it's
      1) cheap Backgrounds,
      2) only reliably obtainable at character creation, combined with
      3) the prioritization of character survival above all other goals (as was outlined in the introductory section) that's problematic.

      Even if the Storyteller were to have some means of hitting your Backgrounds, if survival is still your #1 goal, then it should still be your top priority.

      However, say, if we're using the sample campaign and I say "okay, you all must have some motivation to stop the invasion of Chicago vampires and their influences into your town", that gives us a different-than-survival priority to put at the center of the game, and Backgrounds - even cheap Backgrounds only reliably obtainable at character creation - are not a problem then. You are absolutely right they are GREAT for character differentiation, niche protection, verisimilitude, escapism, all kinds of good things come from Backgrounds.

      \n I'll chime in and echo that heavy handed GMing at the beginning is critical to a story-esque collaboration during the actual play. I carefully followed the direction to have solo preludes, and take a heavy hand in character creation, along with saying firmly no when a concept or build didn't fit.

      As a result, we had four well anchored characters, with a lot at stake (pardon the pun) in the direction of the chronicle. If I'd had the scene framing skills then that I do now, I can only imagine how much better those games would've been.

      As a result, we had several year plus campaigns that were well loved by everyone, that actually approached what many in the Forge influenced diaspora would recognize as nascent Narativist play.

      \n\nEero_Tuovinen \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nOctober 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n My experience of traditional rpgs is that there is a massive pre-textual model of roleplaying you develop yourself and then apply to the different games. This is apparently caused in part by the game texts themselves, as the attitude seems to rise independently in different places; it's not a difficult leap to speculate that the lack of stridency and procedural absoluteness that e.g. boardgames have for how to play right has a big role here in teaching players to approach all games as extensions of their general craft instead of coming to new games with a fresh palate (as is the norm with the current proge design and play culture).

      With this feature of the trad rpg culture in mind I personally would not be surprised if much that is associated with Vampire is actually just the dominant traditional rpg culture as viewed through the Vampire lens; it is perceived as a flagship of '90s game design, so it's no wonder if people transfer their general '90s associations to the game. It's just one game text, and of course it can influence how you play, but if it doesn't actually shake you up and force you to pay attention, and especially if it encompasses multitudes and refuses to take stands, then it's natural that you're going to just play it the same way you play any other games. We never played Vampire around here in the '90s, but the games we did play suffered (or benefited, however you want to view it) of this phenomenon very strongly: mechanics and settings changed, but the game we played was procedurally and creatively the same.

      If you accept this characterization of how the rpg culture was at one point and how the game texts supported that culture, then it is entirely consistent to simply not be up in arms against Vampire - it doesn't seem to me to be any more flawed than any other games being published at the time. Personally I even couldn't mobilize against it as I'm not that familiar with the game. It's just one of an endless series of formula designs (not in that its writing and ideas weren't unique, but rather that the systemic design is both standard and vague - the traditional era of rpg design has brought us many incomparable pieces of rpg writing, even with the issues in game design) that the tradition churned out during the '85-'00 era. To measure the game's specific gravity we'd need to get it into the hands of people with no prior chance to develop their own approach to roleplaying, and even that has its own problems due to how Vampire doesn't seem like a beginner's game at all.

      All that being said, I'd like to thank Jason for the retrospective. I've decided that I'll have to get the book and check out Vampire myself. Now that I have ascended into a veritable rpg guru (comparing to my teenage self from the '90s, you understand), I'm all too interested in playing even games that I'd have passed on earlier; so what if Vampire requires the GM to be pretty autocratic in places, it might still be an interesting and fresh experience that I'm still lacking! I've admired the setting for a long time anyway, I like the Caine myth and all the stuff it manages to wring out of vampires. Good fiction, and perhaps I could manage to play the game, too, with the aid of latter days rpg wisdom.

      Also, a bit about rpg theory: a cohesive vision of the scenario you start play with is a key element of modern narrativist design. Jason's point that this is what Vampire actually advices is interesting in how it emphasizes my above point: when we hear from the many people who've found the game problematic, we're apparently hearing from people who either never applied this structure to their play, or tried and failed due to missing the right player postures and other details. This isn't too peculiar to me as a part of rpg history, as that history is riddled with the pedagogical challenge of rpg writing: how to actually make people play the game correctly without being there yourself to correct their mistakes, and how to do this when simultaneously commercial success requires non-committal writing. Evidently a program like having a consistent campaign arc vision and making players play along with that was too much, too softly communicated to really get through when Vampire was published. Perhaps the exact same game text would be read in a functional manner by more players today than it was at the time, due to how our relationships to game texts and our expectations of play techniques have changed.

      (If it seems like I'm not as ultimately interested in game texts as practices, that's just a logical progression in my interests now that I've been wrangling with D&D for five years. Nothing to help you realize the ultimately subjective relationship game texts have to the genuine game as trying to play D&D as it truly is behind all those slightly differing texts.)

      \n Great insights into how play culture may have affected how people played, and I definitely agree that it was a product of its times, especially mechanically. The die pool mechanic had roots in Shadowrun (1989) and Humanity in morality mechanics like Pendragon's (1985). It wasn't until later editions that economies like Humanity, the Blood Pool, and Backgrounds were really balanced with each other/set up properly, it was still pretty rough at this point, particularly the ability to regain Humanity.

      \n The only mechanical way to increase a Background is through Diablerie, by which you obtain Generation. Everything else is "if your Storyteller says okay, you can increase a Background". I'll give you the text in a bit. It's a mess.

      It also caused a mess around this in my local play culture.

      Some people said "You can be filthy rich with no Resources dots due to your great play, bold moves, deals with the devil, whatever. Backgrounds are just what they say on the label - backgrounds ONLY. Once we start playing, they don't mean shit - your Contacts might feed you bad info, your Allies might work for the other side, IF you don't treat them right."

      Some people said all that and added: "AND I actually put and take away dots on your character sheet and if that means you fuck up and I eliminate ALL your backgrounds - your allies are dead, your money was stolen or spent, your status is trashed, some mystical doodad is thinning your blood so your Generation sucks...then you are sitting there with no dots in your Background section and the dude next to you who just started the game gets his 7 just like before. TOO BAD, SUCKER, play better next time."

      Some people (including me) said, "What you pay for at character creation, you basically keep. These are core elements of your character. Physical stuff that you buy with these points are eternal, so are the Merits/Flaws, so whatever happens, it's not legit for me to wreck shit that's the core of your character. You may be alone in your giant mansion stripped bare of all furniture sobbing and screaming about how you were right, why can't the fools see that you were right all along, but you are gonna keep that mansion if you paid for that mansion." (I developed this point of view after the Player's Guide came along with the Merit/Flaw system purchased out of the same pool - this was what basically convinced me that Backgrounds shouldn't be reduced past their initial starting place.)

      Other people just said "I can't figure out why there's not just an XP cost associated with these, if you do Appropriate Things and spend XP, you can get everything else on your character sheet, clearly this is just a mistake. Backgrounds should be like everything else - you prioritize it in play and then you spend XP to put it on your sheet where it's yours. Similarly I can take it away, but whatever, it's no big you can buy it back eventually."

      Some backgrounds are indeed rolled as bonuses, others provide characters to boss around (allies) or get information from (contacts). Since they are drawn up as NPCs with their own die pools these are actually not bad investments.

      I think you've got a good, viable way of interpreting it...maybe it's even in there somewhere and it's my own memory of how I played that's messing it up! I'll post the text about it soon, see what you think.

      (But yeah, the Disciplines are straight up a good thing.)

      \n I'm happy that Eero touched on play culture of the time playing a part in all of this.

      It means I don't have to!

      The problems I had trying to run a VtM campaign can pretty much be traced to this, as well as my own failure to be more invlved with those early set ups for the campaign and skipping the running of preludes as written.

      Frankly, even with some forethought about what I wanted in a campaign, I still tended to follow the older tradition of simply allowing players to make whatever they wanted character-wise with very few restrictions, and not a whole lot of guidance on bigger picture issues.

      I'll forgive myself on that last point. All of the people I was playing with had some background in RPGs. We were used to the idea that players simply made whatever they rolled/created and we'd make it work.

      Which does kinda-sorta work for teams of heroic(ish) adventurers of one sort or another, and was pretty much the style of play for everything up to that point which we'd encountered.

      Not so good with VtM though. I guess we weren't the only ones doing it. There's a reason that Superheroes with Fangs is a common description for play at the time.

      On a side note, and not to grind up too much outdated terminology, but I never thought VtM was supposed to be a Narrativist game. Even before I saw folks using the term, I always assumed this was supposed to be Sim:Genre Emulation, just one stuck with a core of the general style of semi-Gamist/Sim: Physics Engine hybrid rules that were common at the time.

      \n\nEero_Tuovinen \n\n\n

      \n The rhetorics of the game smell of narrativism pretty strongly. This idea of setting up vampire characters who need to learn to adapt to their condition, and the idea that you'll let players play their characters as people to see what they'll do, it's all strongly narrativist. People with more familiarity tell me that Vampire doesn't function coherently in that regard, though, and I've no reason to disbelieve; games I know from the same era often had very strong rhetoric about literary pretensions while being mechanically completely bare of any sort of creative agenda. I don't usually analyze most pre-Forge games in CA terms exactly because games used to be written so vaguely when it came to creative issues; CA depended much more on how you played than what you played. I'm sure that the Vampire play of the time ranged all over the creative map even where groups found a locally coherent and fun way to play. The games with clear creative agenda were an exception then, not a rule.

      If we were to nitpick the agenda incoherence of the Vampire text, it wouldn't be difficult to point out creative imprimaturs that go any which way in there. I'm personally stricken by the strong gamist wibe in some of Jason's quotes: the game seems to instruct the players to consider the vampire life as a battleground where their job is to ensure that their characters end up as winners. Based on reading some later WW games (primarily Exalted) I'm inclined to think that this is just a bare design mistake; the writer attempts to motivate and orient players by talking tough game, perhaps, all without realizing how this could potentially mess with the Storyteller's attempts at building a sensitive and thematically aware group activity. Or maybe he simply doesn't have verbiage for any other attitudes that players might bring to a game, I've seen that too. Either way, the player and GM instructions have often been very much at odds in WW games, with players being encouraged to game the system and GMs encouraged to focus on prepping storylines (as opposed to challenges, which one would expect if the game was looking to intentionally go gamist). And that's just the gamist/narrativist issue, there's clearly a lot of simulationist rhetoric in there, too.

      But as said, I at least am not one to consider it an immense sin on the part of an individual game book that it's vague and all over the place when the book hails from 20 years back; it was a different time. I've been known to say some very strict things to people who consider publishing this type of game text now, but we're living a different time with different expectations. If a designer writes or a company publishes a '90s style trad game today, that's clearly a creative or commercial choice and not just a piece of history we're looking at in hindsight. (And to be clear, it's a choice that disinterests me personally, and I don't usually bother with trad games, but that doesn't mean you shouldn't do it if you like this type of game writing.)

      \n Gonna reply to the question about source material and the question about "narrativism" (ugh) at the same time, I think it's the same answer. Anyone that looked at Vampire and thought it would give us what-we-now-think-of-as-narrativism was simply straight drunk. Morality in Vampire stories is just part of the genre, to be kicked around and celebrated with all the verve, and depth, of lace cuffs and silver bullets with crosses carved in them. It is part of the scenery, to be chewed. You can't really have horror without some kind of moral strictures/world. Vampire posits a completely arbitrary and Victorian moral world and then says "do what you want - either try to claw your way back to humanity/Golconda, or descend into madness, or stick it out with politics, whatever, lol" There really isn't anything in it that says you, the player, will be making moral statements regarding Egri-premises when you play - Storytellers bring themes and premises to the table, but in the normal sense of those words, not the highly specialized Egri sense of those words.

      Because narrativism is defined so narrowly (it is a useless concept, NO WAIT THIS ISNT THAT THREAD), you really are never, ever going to "luck into it", unless someone just really likes your game and puts it in the Narrativism essay for no reason, like Castle Falkenstein? And certainly not in a game that is simultaneously trying to let you be rowdy rock and roll antiheroes and struggling blood addicts trying to keep from losing their family.

      (Interestingly, the "get back to being human" part was de-emphasized DRASTICALLY in 2nd Edition and Revised. Like, to the point where I don't remember if it got more than a paragraph in either book, whereas it's all over 1st and has a whole Chronicle Concept based around it.)

      Source material is layered throughout with extensive quotes from songs, books, poetry (haha) at the beginning of chapters and sections. But any Anne Rice fan will instantly recognize the crap in Vampire 1ed, with delight (it's actually a lot more well-thought-out than Rice's world, that is to say, not well thought out at all.) Other than these references, the text really tries to hit you with mood rather than with specific allegories or parallels.

      Anyway, you can buy Vampire: Bloodlines for $5 on Steam right now, go drink blood from rats and remember, these are the days of cellphone cameras. Fuckups ain't tolerated.

    How to play Vampire: the Masquerade, First Edition

    \n
    \xab1234567\xbb

      I have friends that regularly brag of LARPS: "We didn't use the rules all game".

      This is connected to that. There's a big huge playstyle disjunction that happens all the time in groups, it happens right here, and it's not even remotely unique to Vampire.
      \nAgreed, not remotely unique. Just more frequent and more disappointing. In D&D, my badassery wasn't defined as social, and social conflicts were rare. In Vampire, social badassery was important, and social conflicts were frequent. And social conflict is probably the number 1 hotbed of the above disjunction.

      \n
      Actually the jerks...sort of have a point
      \nWell, totally invalidating somebody's character choices is a pretty dick move, but you can strike a balance between honouring them and the fiction both. Like on the one side, starting a chronicle with one PC's 3-dot haven burning down, or having their contacts only tell them lies really sucks from a player perspective. But if you make an ally a central character and the vampire brings them along on a meet and can't prevent them from getting killed? That's their fault. Also, that's drama. On the opposite end of bad play, though, if the ST kept that ally inviolate no matter what, somebody would okay might just buy a bunch of allies and wear them like bullet-proof vests.

      Meanwhile, buying backgrounds with xp is kinda cool, just like buying a gang in AW is cool, but if you can't, there's more motivation to pursue stuff in the fiction. Again, as long as your ST isn't a dick about taking your stuff away and is concerned with making your choices matter.

      But that's "functional play" stuff and not "what does the text say" stuff.

      \n I rather like the idea of Backgrounds not being stagnant. When we played Vampire (more-so Hunter) in the 90's, Backgrounds weren't truly stagnant, but the ST rarely touched them - mostly because we didn't think to. It would obviously be a dick move to take away Background points - unless the player chose to risk those background elements during play in exchange for a payoff. So maybe a character uses one of their allies as bait for a nasty werewolf or something. They're putting their background at risk so they don't have to put themselves at risk. I wouldn't feel bad on either side of the table if the bait got creamed and the Background point was lost. As a player, I'd feel that it was better my Background point getting slashed then my face.
      \n

      \n\nJDCorley \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n So going back, I realized I way over-promised what would be "next" and then got wasted on Halloween and now have forgotten everything I promised. FUCK IT let's do this. I'm going to continue on with the Storyteller instructions first, because really these are pretty good.

      Here's some Do's and Don'ts from that chapter:\n
      \u2022 Keep all the characters in mind . Provide something for everyone to do. no matter what the Scene. Even if one Character is the one on whom everything rests. cut off your roleplaying with that player after a bit and do something with the other players. It may not make any difference to the plot but it will let everyone have some fun, keep them from being bored, and will make them a part of the Scene. Let every player have an equal chance to do something, to take an action or interact with one of your characters. Though you may naturally give more time to the more creative and inventive roleplayers in your group, never ignore someone else.

      \u2022 Go beyond the rules. The rules are for keeping Characters in line. If your imagination is superior to the rules, then go beyond the rules. Especially for non-player Vampire powers, you should let your imagination roam free. There are Werewolves and Wizards and even Ghosts in this world, yet in this game they are not carefully detailed. You'll have to make up their powers on your own. You may make them as mysterious and enigmatic as you desire. Future products in the Storyteller\u2122 series will detail these beings more completely.

      \u2022 Encourage the players to roleplay among them\xadselves. You want to see real and vivid interaction between the players. If they don't roleplay unless they're talking to you, you know something is wrong.

      \u2022 Take Breaks. Sometimes you're going to need to take a break. To stand back from the action and take a look at where the story is going and where it's at. At such times, simply
      stand back from the table and take a short walk, or open this book and pretend you are reading. Don't feel embarrassed about this; everyone needs these rests. The players get them all
      the time. but the Storyteller almost never does. It's almost impossible to sustain a high level of dynamic storytelling without breaks. A good idea is to start the players roleplaying with one another before you rest, since this ensures that they aren't bored while you're not involved.

      \u2022 Encourage Player Input. Don't run the game with\xadout being aware of what your players like and don't like. Balance the desire of the players to achieve the objectives of their characters with their desire to roleplay - let them accomplish their goals without breaking character.
      \nAll pretty great except for 'go beyond the rules', which makes more sense when you interpret it to mean that the rules are there to keep the characters in line with each other and other vampires.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nJDCorley \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Here are the Don'ts:\n
      The following are things which you should never do:

      \u2022 Don't take away the characters' free will. Players are invariably protecti ve of what they consider to be the free will of their characters. They want real choices, and the freedom to choose their characters' actions. While they need to feel that if they do something stupid they will be punished, you don't want them to feel that, if they think of something smart, you will punish them, because it wasn't what you had anticipated.

      \u2022 Don't force the characters into a pre-determined plot. In Vampire. more so than in other games. the characters need to be self-motivated. You cannot simply stick them into your story. You have to weave the story around them and let them become involved in it at their own pace. Often this means you must create the story as you go. making up each Scene and doing a great deal of impromptu roleplaying. It is difficult. but fulfilling.

      \u2022 Don't let luck rule the plot. Characters should get to win on the basis of their own skill and ingenuity, not on unrelated dice rolls. Try to give the players every chance to have the experience and Abilities of their characters make the difference. Automatic successes are an excellent way to emphasize how important their ratings are.

      \u2022 Don't cater to stereotypes. Though you will use stereotypes regularly. you should always try to twist or change them. Stereotypes are excellent tools for the Storyteller, since they allow you to quickly get across a character or a situation. Put a stereotype into a story, play it like a stereotype for most of the story (outraging the players in the process), and then. near the conclusion, suddenly flip everything upside down by breaking apart the stereotype. As an extreme example, the evil snarling villain can tum out to be a simple-minded Nosferatu as terrified of the characters as they are of him. It can be very effective and pleasantly educational.

      \u2022 Don't ignore the characters' Traits. It is important to be be aware of the characters' Traits, especially those flaws which the players so often often "forget" such as Derangements. Some Traits that you are not aware of can sometimes become relevan tto the story and make your planed resolution inconsistent or unrealistic. It is vital you have a good grasp of who and what
      the characters are.

      For instance. you might spring a trap on the characters, roll the damage dice, and start describing wounds, only to have one of the players say, "My character has Premonitions, I get to roll to see if he sensed danger." Even if he misses the roll, it ruins the momentum you had established and damages the story that you were weaving. Look over the characters before the game and make notes of things you should remember.
      \nI include that last example because I think it's very instructive. You mustn't make the characters abilities useless - indeed, you MUST ruin your momentum and damage your story if you negligently do it, according to these principles - so don't be negligent and pay attention to character capability, make those the lynchpins of the action.
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n\nJackFractal \n\n\n

      \n These are interesting!

      They remind me a lot of the Principals section in Apocalypse World. Does anyone remember any further white wolf products having sections like this? \n
      Go beyond the rules. The rules are for keeping Characters in line. If your imagination is superior to the rules, then go beyond the rules. Especially for non-player Vampire powers, you should let your imagination roam free. There are Werewolves and Wizards and even Ghosts in this world, yet in this game they are not carefully detailed. You'll have to make up their powers on your own. You may make them as mysterious and enigmatic as you desire. Future products in the Storyteller\u2122 series will detail these beings more completely.
      \nThis one could be easily misinterpreted to mean "the rules are for keeping the players in line". It doesn't mean that, but I could seem my fifteen-year-old self getting confused by that. \n
      Don't force the characters into a pre-determined plot. In Vampire. more so than in other games. the characters need to be self-motivated. You cannot simply stick them into your story. You have to weave the story around them and let them become involved in it at their own pace. Often this means you must create the story as you go. making up each Scene and doing a great deal of impromptu roleplaying. It is difficult. but fulfilling.
      \nThis seems to directly contradict previous advice on planning. Before it was suggesting that you should plan out almost everything before-hand, and ensure that your players did not create characters that would be disruptive to your carefully prepared schemes. Now it's suggesting that you play reactively.

      This is the kind of mutually contradictory advice that really messed with my head when I tried to play these when I was in high-school.

      \n
      \n\nCaptain_Thark \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Ok, so it's not that contradictory. You should have figured out before play starts:
      *How the players fit together
      *How the vampire politics in the city the game is set in work.
      *What motivations everybody has
      *What sort of schemes Vamps are up to
      *How other supernatural types will play into this, if at all
      *What state the city is in because of this.

      The question is what happens when the players interact with this. Because you as the GM have answers to these questions. You don't know what will happen if the PCs manage to trap the leader of the Sabbat in a locked room doused in gasoline and light it on fire. You know who liked them, you know who hated them, and you know how the PCs will be viewed.

      I love City of the Damned: New Orleans for Requiem because it's a great example of all of this. Everybody links to the Prince of the city in some way, and the twist is the Prince is about to be taken out of the picture no matter what very soon. So everybody wants to put the next Prince on the throne, and you also know how everybody fits together. Plug the PCs in, and you're ready for some good times.
      \n

      \n Jack, if you replace "plot" with "PC actions" and "story" with "situation," like Jason suggested upthread, it makes more sense. Also, I think it's suggesting that you plan theme, tone, and NPCs during character creation, and then how you introduce it to the characters, who are created specifically to engage with the the theme and tone, is based on PC actions. The books doesn't seem to distinguish between "stuff you prep" and "how you introduce situation to the players," calling it all "story."\n
      All pretty great except for 'go beyond the rules', which makes more sense when you interpret it to mean that the rules are there to keep the characters in line with each other and other vampires.
      \nThat part looks to me like it just means that PCs only get to choose from the skills and disciplines listed in the book, the NPCs can have whatever powers the Storyteller wants. So when you create NPC vampires, they're not restricted to what the rules say characters can do.

      \n Now I really want to see somebody grab Vampire 2nd edition for some comparative forensics. Has the WW house vision of how to play roleplaying games stayed stable, or has it moved to some direction since this seminal game text?

      \n Well, City of the Damned: New Orleans fits this model exactly and it's from Requiem, which is basically the 'fourth' edition. I will say first edition is waaaay more clear about this than later, but strong threads of it stick around in Requiem. It's kind of like the difference between the instructions on how to play in Red Box D&D vs the AD&D Dungeon Master's guide. They are both advise for the same thing, it's just clearer in Red Box D&D and First Edition Vampire.

      \n\nLisa Padol \n\n\n

      \n\nNovember 2012 \n

      \n
      Oh, shit, y'all just reminded me of one of my least favorite Vampire experiences. I roleplayed the hell out of negotiating an alliance with a member of another clan, and the scene was pretty much done and the agreement made, and I didn't think I'd have to roll (largely because we didn't roll for that when we played D&D, but also because we hadn't rolled for other social actions in this same session) and then the Storyteller's like, "Okay, Dave, roll Manipulation." Oh, that sucked!

      Is this a game about getting into character and roleplaying your ass off and sticking with the dramatic spirit represented by the book's flavor? Or is this a game where you buy some stats and roll some dice and get what you pay for? Rrrgh
      \nI've hit this problem with other games, particularly Burning Wheel / Mouse Guard, and folks had problems with it in Panty Explosion. Fortune in the Middle is the solution recommended to me.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nLisa Padol \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I had a love / hate relationship with Vampire: The Masquerade, which meant I bought a lot of the books. Things I didn't like were, I think, more related to the scenarios.

      There's one, I think Milwaukee by Night? It says, at least twice, something on the order of (not a direct quote) "It's bad to take away player agency and bad to say the PCs are captured no matter what. But, sometimes, it's absolutely necessary, like right here in this scenario..."

      Bullshit. It is not necessary.

      And I got so sick of (again, not a direct quote or anything remotely resembling one) "Okay, so your players have paid dots for Haven and Auspex and the like, and should be able to spot anyone following them and keep their havens secret. Because, really, this is, you know, like, a matter of unlife and death. But, despite that, No Matter What, the enemy of the scenario has totally located and trashed all of their havens, and they never saw him coming."

      Okay. And, then the PC must do X and go to Y location where Z will happen, and I'm playing why? Just write your novel and send it to me.

      No, Vampire was not the only offender. Don't get me started on Tribe 8. But, no, this was not the only way to write a scenario, even in the 1990s. In the 1990s, I was running Call of Cthulhu scenarios, many of which were excellent when it came to not saying "No Matter What". I presume there were other games with other scenarios that avoided "And here's what will happen, no matter what those pesky PCs try to do." Call of Cthulhu is just the one I fell in love with.

      I really got ticked with the Giovanni Chronicles. I'm not just talking the usual railroading, though that's there in spades. In the second one, No Matter What, someone they're likely trying to protect will be killed, giving himself up if necessary to keep the PCs from protecting him. But, what really annoyed me? In the first three, the PCs were basically pawns. Same PCs, and by the time of the fourth one, they would have been really powerful. So, there's payoff for playing through all of this, right? Wrong! Part four explains that the PCs are too powerful to play, so the players are now playing far weaker characters, pawns once more. Bait and switch.

      The game rules themselves? I read them, winced at the ones I didn't like, and ignored the stuff I thought was ridiculous. Friends did likewise, resulting in a game we all enjoyed. But, I do not and never have accepted "Well, just ignore what you don't like and add anything you think is missing" as proof that a game is not flawed.

      It was an important game, and it taught me some useful tricks. I think it's where I learned the concept of a relationship map and got it so ingrained that Ron Edwards and I had several rounds of "But, I didn't make a relationship map" / "Yes, Lisa, you totally did -- now go run your game already".

      And meanwhile, I ignored some stuff so hard that when Mike Holmes told me about the GM -- excuse me, Storyteller -- who did a four hour prelude where Mike had no agency whatsoever, my first mental reaction was, "Oh, you poor guys! You actually believed that crap and tried to do it!" I mean, they were following one perfectly valid interpretation of the Prelude rules: Do it one on one if possible. Don't give the player much choice.

      And I think that's a lot of it, communication issues. A four hour Prelude doesn't need to suck.

      There's something to the theory that Vampire had a strong effect because of the mess it was. And, it was a language that was spoken nationwide. When I was in line for registration for the San Francisco WorldCon, the people in front of me were talking about the Tremere, and I knew whereof they spoke. One gets that with D&D and World of Darkness. What else is that big these days? (I'm not sure World of Darkness currently is.)

      \n\nAccountingForTaste \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n edited November 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      In the 1990s, I was running Call of Cthulhu scenarios, many of which were excellent when it came to not saying "No Matter What". I presume there were other games with other scenarios that avoided "And here's what will happen, no matter what those pesky PCs try to do." Call of Cthulhu is just the one I fell in love with.
      \nI know exactly what you mean! Seriously, Call of Cthulhu had some ridiculously amazing scenarios published for it. I think I only ever encountered two or three that I didn't adore.

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nMike_Holmes \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n To be clear, about the incident of which Lisa was relating, I had no idea that there was such a thing as a "Prelude." Perhaps if I'd been told that there would be one, I'd have come back for a second session. But Lisa has the details right, it was a full session where I had literally zero player power. I would be asked, questions that implied that I could do other than what the Storyteller wanted (also named Mike, as it happens). Like "Do you go along?" But if I answered, "I try to run" I was told, "You try, but you simply can't. You follow him against your will."

      I suppose even the attempt to go against the GM's plot is a form of interaction, a statement about my character's character. But even those were far and few between. As I told Lisa, mostly I remember the GM telling me about details like how red the velvet curtains were, now that I could truly see them with my newly vampire eyes.

      And lest you think that this was just a prima donna GM, he even talked about how "advanced" this form of play was, Storytelling, and how evolved it all was from earlier sorts of play. He was a true believer, a convert to the cause.

      And... yeah, it was a leap forward in thinking in terms of the notion that play could be about things dramatic. It WAS about very dramatic stuff. It just wasn't participatory. At least not in this case.

      Note, too, that this was not the only time that I had this happen. I tried it again with another GM, and got a very similar vibe. Lots of the stuff that Lisa is talking about, actually. "Sure, your vampire is really cool... but he sucks compared to all the other ones." That was the paradigm in every single game I played. If the players had cool characters, the GM (excuse me, Storyteller) had much cooler characters, and it was his job to make sure you knew how cool they were. I believe that this must have been encoded in some of the sample early scenarios, and it stuck. Or that sure seemed to be the case. In any case, the vastly more powerful elders meant that the GM got to have his plot, and the players were happily railroaded along. Or else.

      Every. Single. Game. I never played one functional (read fun) game of VtM. Even when we used GURPs.

      Your milage will vary, I'm sure. But to say that the texts didn't cause this sort of play seems... well just wrong. It might not have in every case, but it did in so many.

      \n
      \n Maybe it was a problem that the appeal the book pitched was largely the appeal of being a player, but the person who bought the book usually wound up as Storyteller? So, you have all these people using GMing as their surrogate for playing? Thus, "look at my awesome character, let's do a story about them" becomes "look at my awesome NPCs, this is a story about them"?

      \n\nJDCorley \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n edited November 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Some definitely did this, against the direct instruction of the main rulebook, and much of its mechanics!

      (But, as people have noted, if they took the "how this is to be done" from the modules of the day, which is not unreasonable, then yes, they absolutely would drive it off that cliff, along with many others. By the way, Call of Cthulhu isn't invulnerable to this either, especially modules of the era. One might take your cues, say, from the Gaslight 2ed scenario (1989), in which investigators who say they are suspicious of a suspicious character and try to keep guard over them wake up - no roll - in bed after being drugged. That would lead you to a pretty crap Call of Cthulhu experience, yes, directly against the instructions of the Call of Cthulhu rulebook.)

      \n Actually CoC had some of the worst modules of all time. I often cite "At the Mountains of Madness" as the worst example of this, as it's basically a 400 page script for the players to follow. It actually says stuff like "When the players give chase..." and has advice in one scene on how to use a magician's force to ensure that a scene comes out like it's supposed to.

      But even the less horrific ones suffer from the problem that you can't possibly get through them unless the GM cheats. At some point the players will all fail some roll, and that's the end of the adventure, unless the GM "cheats" somehow to keep it alive. This necessity becomes a hard-core teaching method to get GMs to understand how to use illusionism and all sorts of force to railroad.

      Or that's what it did to me. Note that I'm not saying "Well they were all bad, except for me." I was this GM, too. For two decades.

      Now I really want to see somebody grab Vampire 2nd edition for some comparative forensics. Has the WW house vision of how to play roleplaying games stayed stable, or has it moved to some direction since this seminal game text?
      \nI don't have it handy, but I checked the Dark Ages rulebook and the Storyteller's main duties -- like play the NPCs, describe the setting, adjudicate the rules, and put the characters in situation (the "story"!) -- are pretty clearly labelled, and it's clear that the PCs are the main thing that it all revolves around. How much of that sinks in after you've read the preceding 220 pages, though...

      \n As to Call of Cthulhu: I dunno nuthin about the Mountains of Madness or Cthulhu by Gaslight, never bought them, never played them, but I've run and played dozens of other published CoC adventures -- including old classics, including the Orient express one which is a literal AND figurative railroad -- and never once ran into a situation where continuing the scenario hinged on a single roll. Never. There were always enough clues lying around to keep going.

      \n Johnstone, I include rolls for survival in this, not just clues. I believe it was in Masks of Nyarlathotep in which the first part of the scenario was to climb up a wall of collapsing glacial ice, to get details of something carved in a rock (which, due to the collapse of the ice had to be inspected immediately, or be lost to the murky depths underneath permanently. I recall two thirds of the party dying there, due to bad rolls. First scene. We decided to reboot the scenario, and just do it over. Only one PC died in this case, but apparently that was enough. Between the rebooting and dying, nobody was interested in continuing to play the scenario. Just didn't seem right.

      Note that this was what happened if you didn't "fudge" and figure out some way to have the PCs survive. My GM, Jim, was a stickler for playing by the rules (no, not a "killer GM" just the most honest person anyone has ever met). To whit, in another scenario run by Jim, my friend Dave had his character die by having his head blown off by fumbling a roll with fishing with dynamite. In yet another scenario, Dave's character died falling off a ladder. Ooooh... scary mythos-y stuff there. After these events Dave swore never to play the game again, and never returned. In playing one of the classic haunted house scenarios, the players were expected from the clues to figure out that it was not the ebony mantlepiece that was involved in a dream they were having, but that it was the oak mantlepiece that was involved, due to figuring out that the shadowy figures in the dream were druids. We simply didn't figure that out as players. A girl (oh so cute, as I recall) who was new to RPGs swore off playing them forever, because the scenario just ended with us walking away unable to figure it out.

      I'm not making this stuff up. I've played most of the published scenarios, at least through the first scene. I've read them all since then. Yes, it's possible to work around these issues. But they came up in every single scenario that I played of CoC. Note that when I went and played with the Rogue Cthulu folks at Origins or similar groups at GenCon, they figured out good ways around these issues. The plots were substantially railroaded, but at least you were guaranteed to get to the scary stuff, and likely have your character killed. This was another interesting functional part of the con scenarios for CoC... you could have your character act "appropriately stupid" (to quote the RPG "It Came From the Late, Late, Late Show"), and die, and feel that you'd added to the game. Instead of having your character die from something random in the scenario that you were trying hard to "win", and feeling really, really dumb about it.

      CoC scenario design has always had these problems. Yes, some GMs have tricks for getting around them. A typical one is using the magician's force for library use die rolls. "Oh, you missed the roll? Then you only find these few meagre clues." Or, "Oh, you made the roll? You find this bonanza of clues after a really hard dig." Either way giving them the same information. Or, even better, inventing a new and harder or riskier way to get the information. Bad methods included having NPCs walk up and tell you the clue you missed. Some GMs were better than others at this.

      In any case, the point is the same. What this did, for GMs like myself, was to teach us to use illusionism to ensure that the scenario got played out. Or, in the case of my buddy Jim, it meant losing players in short order because the game wasn't fun. Yeah, I'm sure that somewhere there's a group of masochists who love NOT being able to figure out mysteries (or, OK, the reward of being successful when they do), and really love it when their characters die pointlessly. But I personally haven't met these folks. Nor can I stand personally being a GM for such a game.

      The Gumshoe system, while not exactly to my taste in other ways, is precisely a fix of these problems. And in that respect it does it's job well. Robin Laws would not have created this system had he not seen the same issues in CoC play. He's said this explicitly, and you can probably find it online somewhere.
      \n

      \n\nMike_Holmes \n\n\n

      \n\nNovember 2012 \n

      \n
      \n Double posting...

      In case anyone reading doesn't know what "Illusionism" means, it is a GMing style that intends to leave the players feeling that they have actual agency in the outcome of a scenario, when in fact the GM is railroading the scenario to a pre-planned conclusion. Another common technique is termed "all roads lead to Rome" where at the fork in the road, it doesn't matter which way the players decide to go, the GM decides that what he needs to be there for the scenario to progress as planned is at the one selected.

      The problem with illusionism is that the players know you're doing it when you're doing it. Oh maybe not on X roll, or Y decision. But over time they'll figure it out. Things just work out too conveniently. If things were really "random" you'd end up with results like my friend Jim's GMing. Players aren't dumb, they're on to an illusionist GM almost immediately. Some players just go along with this. But for those who understand that they don't have actual agency, and who really want it, this can really kill their fun.

      Cost me two decades of time running bad games, and I couldn't figure out what I was doing wrong. Surely I could figure out how to ensure that a scenario would get to it's end, and yet allow the players to be creating the story at the same time? I mean, after all, the book said I could do it... Illusionism is the best I - or anyone I met during the 80s and 90s - could do.

      Jack, if you replace "plot" with "PC actions" and "story" with "situation," like Jason suggested upthread, it makes more sense.
      \nRight, but if we want to play the word substitution game then we can quickly make the game about super powered cupcakes.

      I wont argue that it's impossible to parse this text, using a wide experience with other games, and extract valuable information... but I will say that without that wide experience it was easy to read this text and create dysfunctional play.

      And yes, the text IS contradictory.

      This: \n
      [as the storyteller] a plan of the progression of the Chronicle should be constructed, to detail where you want to take it.
      \nIs DIRECTLY contradicted by this: \n
      Don't force the characters into a pre-determined plot. In Vampire. more so than in other games. the characters need to be self-motivated. You cannot simply stick them into your story. You have to weave the story around them and let them become involved in it at their own pace. Often this means you must create the story as you go. making up each Scene and doing a great deal of impromptu roleplaying. It is difficult. but fulfilling.
      \nThese are mutually exclusive. You cannot have both.

      \n Hey Bob,

      I didn't mean to derail the thread. But I don't think I have. What I'm saying is that, between the potentially confusing parts of the game texts, and the not-at-all-confusing results of actual play of the scenarios presented, you get what you got at the time. No, it's not just the game texts themselves... those just open the door. It's the entire product. People learn to play games from the scenarios, and you have to include that as part of the product that created the phenomenon.

      Had they not produced the scenarios? Would the text have produced the same results? Well who cares? That's contrary to fact, conditional to error. The point is that it created a culture of play that did these things, as a result of not only these texts, but coming out of a D&D culture, and other facets... I could go on and on as to the reasons.

      Does that mean we can use the same rhetoric today, and get away with it? Well, it's possible. But why would you? There's much better rhetoric to steal from today. What, we're such traditionalists that we have to copy from these old texts? Find a new way.

      In any case, nobody in these parts is being harmed by these texts nowadays, so I'm not particularly worried about them. But the culture of play of which I speak is still alive and well. And I avoid it like the plague.

      \n Jack, I agree there's a lot of bad writing in what Jason's posted and in White Wolf books in general. Those are absolutely contradictory, if you assume that "story" and "plot" and "planning a chronicle" all mean the same thing. There's other passages I could point to that are worse, even, some that don't quite fit Jason's explanation, even.

      But the author obviously didn't think it was contradictory. I mean, he's writing pages and pages of how to play this way. So, is it all bullshit? Is he getting paid by the word? I think he has a definite idea of how the game is supposed to work, but just isn't very good at clearly and concisely articulating it. Some people, like Jason, seemed to have had little or no trouble understanding his intentions, while others, including many people I played with, missed the mark entirely. I think it's a lot more valuable (at least to me) to see where the shortcomings of the text between intent and execution, so we can learn from them, as opposed to harping on the mistakes.

      \n Chronicle and story are not the same thing in this game text. Story and plot are not the same thing in this game text. I know, it's ridiculous, but they're not.

      You DO design a Chronicle with a whip hand, including unrelenting direction to players that they must follow.

      Story is more interactive, but ultimately in the Storyteller's hands - what's the situation (including the character's situation)? What direction is it headed? Be sure you take into account their abilities and motivations when figuring that out. Don't do a bad job and just eliminate all choice, do a good job and work out the situation thoroughly.

      Okay now STOP, because plot is 100 percent in the player's hands. Now you respond to what they do.

      Edit: When I first read this, I was 16 and had only played D&D. And "we ought to do better" is not exactly what the criticism is. The criticism is that Vampire is harmful. Some people think it's so harmful that if you saw me walking towards you with a Vampire book and I looked like I might run a game for you, that you would be justified in shooting me, and no jury ought convict you, because I intended to do you physical harm.

      \n As to what the author's intent matters, I have no idea how you would go about improving game text writing when all you consider is a) the text and b) the poor behaviour of people who read it. I don't know how the brain damage criticism can help me, or anyone else, play better games or write better texts (and mostly for that reason I don't care about the brain damage, at all, it means nothing to me).

      Communicating a functional play style in a way that people actually understand? That helps make better game texts, no question. If there's a functional play style in there behind that text, and we can find better ways to communicate it? Awesome.

      \n
      \n I want to go into some more detail about JackFractal's example because I think the idea of preventing problems in Chronicle design is often underappreciated. Consider the following:

      "You are gonna make Vampire Vigilantes...you don't necessarily have the illusion that you will get drugs off the streets, but your goal is to create a sort of drug-free hunting zone where you can sink your teeth into people and not really have to worry that they popped a ton of shrooms or whatever. If the opportunity for more power comes along, you might or might not take it, think about where you are with respect to your goal when it comes up."

      (I plan for what will happen if they don't take it, because I've told them that's okay.)

      "You are gonna make Vampire Vigilantes...the route to power in other areas is blocked so you are gonna make yourselves the fucking Kingpins. You see the street as the only ecology where you can establish a power base. You are opportunistic motherfuckers."

      (The characters are now overwhelmingly likely to take other opportunities to gain power, though likely to flavor it in their own way, which is great. BTW, this is almost exactly what the Gang Chronicle Concept is in the book. The Storyteller advice mentions what happens if they surprise you and don't go for it: you take a break, think it over, then roll with it.)

      "You are gonna make Vampire Vigilantes. You have your own reasons for that, which we can go into later. But the number of vampires in town is so small that if the five of you wanted to you could easily push out anyone you wanted. There just aren't a ton of people around to get done what needs to get done. Just that when the game starts, you're not looking in that direction (otherwise we'd already be there and you all would be running things.)"

      (This is basically the setup in the sample Chronicle in the book. There are only 7 non-PC vampires in Gary. An assassination/power void/job that's not getting done because the PCs aren't stepping up becomes a really interesting plot development instead of the end of my story.)

      See how Chronicle planning - in conjunction with player cooperation - prevents what JF identified as a story vs. plot problem? That's not a story vs. plot problem, that's a Chronicle planning/communication problem. And also note two of the sample setups actually do address this specific example!
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nMike_Holmes \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n What?

      How can you improve on a text without considering it? And if the majority of people who read the text get the same idea, then they're all just behaving badly? This is typical; if the text doesn't produce functional play, blame the players.

      Do you want an example of how to replace this text with better? I can point you to any number of better examples, including some versions of D&D (and that's not an endorsement). I can write better myself.

      For instance, with regard for the parts quoted most recently about the GM creating his chronicle, and then the players getting to take an active part in moving it forward, one could merely state in addition to the first part about creating a chronicle that one ought not expect it to play out as one may have planned it. That it's a living framework that will necessarily change. Maybe even give tips on how to create such stuff in more provisional fashion, so that you might be less disappointed if/when things don't go the way your planning thought it might.

      Just as an example. But doing things better first requires that we point out what it is that could be done better. No?
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n Actually it DOES say that stories in your Chronicle will not come out how you expect them to, and affirmatively says that if they do, you probably messed up and didn't give the players enough choices.

      There's lots of ways to improve this text, definitely! Nothing on this fallen earth is without flaw.

      \n Well then in your defense of that particular tract you ought to have brought up that point. I don't have the text in front of me, so I'm at a disadvantage. I'm completely willing to stipulate that the effects that I'm citing, that definitely occurred, may have been the product of culture and scenarios, as I've said. But I'm also all for making for better text where possible. Even if this edition of VtM doesn't make the mistake of the impossible thing, it was a definite meme, and it does well to remind folks not to replicate the error in other texts, or to "misread" this text as saying that it's OK to railroad.

      Heck, I'm willing to bet that a large percentage of folks coming to VtM, coming from CoC and such backgrounds like I would have at the time, would not even have read those parts of the game text. Feeling that they already knew how such preparatory things were done. I would probably have been like this. Despite being a bad GM.

      \n\nJohnstone \n\n\n

      \n\nNovember 2012 \n edited November 2012

      \n
      Do you want an example of how to replace this text with better? I can point you to any number of better examples, including some versions of D&D (and that's not an endorsement). I can write better myself.
      \nWhat does your "better" mean? You can't write a text that better expresses the intentions of Vampire's authors unless you understand what those intentions were. D&D is a different game, so how do you define it's texts as better? Because they explain the intent of the authors better? Even if they do, how does that help someone trying to write a different game than D&D, say specifically someone trying to write a game explaining how they played when they played Vampire and it worked? These are some of the questions that come to mind from reading your words. "Better" at what?\n
      For instance, with regard for the parts quoted most recently about the GM creating his chronicle, and then the players getting to take an active part in moving it forward, one could merely state in addition to the first part about creating a chronicle that one ought not expect it to play out as one may have planned it.
      \nIt did say that though, didn't it? Didn't Jason quote that part? Also, if that's not the intention of the game designer, then wouldn't it be a bad idea to put that in? Or does this just make the text better, for you?

      I mean, I'm interested in Jason's explanation of the text because it shows a semi-consistent usage of certain terms in a certain way without clear definitions that I think are confusing to many people. If we look at it and maybe see what the author(s) were intending, and where they went wrong in communicating that to the audience, then we can see how to avoid those same pitfalls by understanding the path from intent to word to interpretation. If we just look at the path from text to interpretation by the reader, what are you supposed to do with that when you sit down to write? That seems like incomplete information to me. If you don't know the intentions behind the words, how do you separate the parts that were interpreted correctly from the parts that weren't?
      \n

      \n\nDeliverator \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n So, I've actually never looked at the text of Vampire before, despite being firmly in the "Vampire is harmful" camp (for reasons of my own, that I can go into if people want). Now that I have, hey guess what? It has exactly the sort of terrible, contradictory bullshit I expected!

      The arguments of the "harmful" camp are, in other words, completely backed up by what's been quoted. However, of course this text alone didn't uniquely determine 90's play culture. So, the question then becomes, why single out Vampire? After all, as has been pointed out, it's hardly worse than a lot of other texts of the era when it comes to the absurd juggling it wants GMs to do.

      I think it's partly that VtM was the dominant, non-D&D game of the era, so we heap scorn on it as representative of general trends we don't like.

      But maybe it's also the way that VtM tried to sell itself as something more literary, deeper, gothier, etc, combined with the poor design and outright terrible writing that lead to a lot of the dysfunction associated with the game. The way it tried to be about something true and terrible but then fell down on the job almost as soon as it got started. That maybe made it worse, as in, more harmful to local play cultures, than many other games of the time.

      Matt
      \n

      \nI keep hearing this, that it promised SO MUCH, but...look at those quotes, up above. It doesn't, really. It just says "storytelling is awesome, you are gonna love this game". It appeals constantly not to theories of drama like Egri, Aristotle or even Syd Field, but to psychotically overblown purple-prose novelist and general trash-purveyor Anne Rice (this is not a criticism of Anne Rice, read all Anne Rice novels, they are hilarious.) Their examples of play are moody violence, not DIEP INTRASPECTION. It just never promised what you think it promised. Except maybe on the back cover copy, I guess? I haven't looked at that yet.\n
      That maybe made it worse, as in, more harmful to local play cultures, than many other games of the time.
      \nNah, it didn't hurt anyone.

      So here is my list of things still to do:

      * Finish Storytelling Chapter
      * Examples of Play
      * Inspirations (? Maybe)

      Then maybe talk about improvements we would make today? I've seen some good ideas so far.

      \n Awesome yet incoherent in my case
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n

      \n\nEero_Tuovinen \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      I find it pretty interesting that Eero is the only person posting whose opinion has actually been swayed by rereading the text. Everyone else is just seeing exactly what they originally saw, whether that be awesomeness or incoherence.
      \nWell it's because I didn't have much of an opinion to begin with. I've just about as much to do with Vampire as I do with something like Harn; I know what it is, have read a good deal about it, but have never even read through a full rulebook. I'm sure that if I had gotten into Vampire like so many people here, then I would also have a more personal opinion on what and how it is. Heck, Call of Cthulhu, there's a game I have opinions and experienced about, and they mainly mirror Mike's.

      An important nuance here is that Jason and Mike and perhaps some other people, too, are having a discussion that started a long time ago. They're essentially fighting the last war: in the early '00s it was an important point of the Forge message that there is something wrong with traditional game design. Disputations change with context, and the context of the time was such that phrasing the things people at the Forge wanted meant largely refuting what had gone before.

      In comparison it seems to me that today we can talk about things in a more neutral manner: I don't think that anybody's opinions have changed any (I still think the same about trad games, for example), but the conclusions and framing of those thoughts is more retrospective now that the Forge movement has largely achieved its aims. We can look at Vampire and say just about what Jason is saying: it's written as the games of its time usually were, apparently the author had a functional play group, the text tries to depict the method but could use some improvements. In practice people read whatever they wanted into it, thanks to the strongly totalizing rpg understanding ("one true way", and everybody had their own) of the time.

      What I'm trying to say is that I read what Mike says and what Jason says, and I don't really disagree with either. They're seeing the game in different lights because they've experienced it in different gaming contexts. It's just the same with me and Call of Cthulhu: we were essentially a cargo cult around here when I learned roleplaying, and the game text combined with our reading of it failed to build us up into a functional CoC group. I'm not wrong when I say that the game was clearly much too vaguely written for us, but then neither probably is Johnstone - I'm sure that his group found a functional way to use that same rules text, which then resulted in functional play.

      Most significantly, I find it a meaningless matter of emphasis whether we say that "Vampire was awfully written and led many astray" or "Vampire worked well for many people". Both of those can be true at the same time. The more interesting question is whether there is something to salvage in the game's teachings.
      \n

And The Burning Wheel and Apocalypse World gave me the mental tools I needed to run trad games in a fun way, since The Burning Wheel defies simple categorization and Apocalypse World is the best damn trad-designed post-apocalyptic game ever!

      So I am a convert, I just converted before getting here.

      Also: Vampire the Masqurade: Bloodlines does a really good job selling Vampire itself. It's all backstaby politics and subterfuge with awesome 90s subculture monsters, not mopey introspective Artistic Important Commentary on the Human Condition.

      \n I think Mike's observations about the culture into which Vampire arrived make a strong case for why better wording was vital. I mean, we all agree that better wording would have been nice. But in a world of railroady CoC GMs, your wording can't just augment their existing habits with a few pointers here and there; you need to actively combat their bad habits. Why? Because these bad habits could ruin their game, and the writers of Vampire should have known that. I mean, the White Wolf guys did play RPGs in the '80s too, right?

      Or maybe I'm setting the bar too high, and CoC was in fact the standard White Wolf was aiming for. Both CoC and Vampire seem to have similar histories of fervent fandom, much awesome play, much awful play, some amazing modules, and some awful modules. If Rein-Hagen was in one of the CoC groups that had an awesome time, maybe he didn't think more was needed. I dunno, still seems like shitty market research at the least.

I was part of a cargo cult myself.

      I think Mike's observations about the culture into which Vampire arrived make a strong case for why better wording was vital. I mean, we all agree that better wording would have been nice. But in a world of railroady CoC GMs, your wording can't just augment their existing habits with a few pointers here and there; you need to actively combat their bad habits. Why?
      \nWorse than why, HOW? Like, the game literally says don't railroad, in many, many different ways, and sets up mechanics to help stop railroading (Disciplines, Backgrounds, etc.) So other than better wording, what else is there?

      \n\nEero_Tuovinen \n\n\n

      \n\nNovember 2012 \n

      \n
      \n Well, I guess you could start a grassroots movement for different gaming, set up a web forum for like-minded gamers, start calling your way of playing something strikingly different and savagely critique different games. This, coupled with patient repeating of your message over and over will then cause people to seriously examine their habits, causing a breakthrough in awareness: instead of just unthinkingly applying their gaming habits to new games they'll start to introspect and intentionally play differently and hey presto, suddenly it is again enough to just say things in the game text because people are in the habit of reading them seriously and doing as they say.

      Admittedly the Internet wasn't very big in '91, so in practice that would be a bit much to ask of the past. They did try, though, what with "Storytelling" and all.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nDavid_Berg \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n edited November 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      Like, the game literally says don't railroad, in many, many different ways, and sets up mechanics to help stop railroading (Disciplines, Backgrounds, etc.) So other than better wording, what else is there?
      \nWay better wording.

      I mean, I doubt I can come up with The Best Solution for Vampire right here and now. But, well, take your favorite instructional game text, the one that's clearest and hardest to inadvertently screw up, and compare Vampire to that. I'm sure you'll find plenty of paths for improvement.

      In the abstract, I really couldn't argue with "the game literally says don't railroad and sets up mechanics to help stop railroading". As you describe it, yeah, it sounds sufficient. But then there are the confusing bits in the text that make anti-railroading less clear, and character Disciplines and Backgrounds don't actually make the GM play in any particular style, and somewhere at the end of all this you have folks with Mike's experiences.

      Off the top of my head, I'd suggest:
      1) Highlight it. Don't just mention "don't railroad" in the middle of 10 paragraphs of advice. Present the points up front, in their own section.
      2) Don't just advise/demand it; show why railroading is no fun and why non-railroading is fun.
      3) Then repeat those points every time you mention any GM task.
      4) Establish an icon (or series of icons) to form a visual connection to each principle that you really need the GM to remember.
      5) Provide actual play examples of a GM being surprised and following the players' lead and it being awesome.
      6) Focus. Shorten the "how to" portion of the book by 87%.
      7) Provide a list of instances where you might railroad without meaning to or even noticing it. Show what to do instead in those instances.

      Or, omit all GM instruction from the core book and then publish an anti-railroading pamphlet called Secrets of the Master Storyteller. Make it pretty, and cheaper than the other Vampire products. You know the fans will buy it.
      \n

      \n 8) Have the final text proofread by a community that is ultra-sensitive to textual suggestions of railroading and extremely hostile to them. (Granted, there probably wasn't one in 1990, or if there was you wouldn't have been able to find it)
