      \n\nJDCorley \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n edited November 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n What's interesting is that White Wolf DID develop a massive Vampire play network based entirely around player-created content before the World Wide Web - the Mind's Eye Theater LARP. Interestingly, I feel it is actually easier for players to "railroad" each other playing by the LARP text than for Storytellers to "railroad" players by the 1ed rulebook text. What is PvP except forcing someone else to give you the outcome you want?

      PS Incoherence is the mark of a good RPG. The more coherent a game is the less player input there is to it. The less player input there is to it, the less player freedom. Basically really focused games are built already-railroaded, but it's the designer railroading the game organizer rather than the game organizer railroading the players. Since designers are distant figures indistinctly understood at worst and completely incommunicado at best, incoherence is better than coherence.

      Am I doin' it right guyz?

      Okay, back to it tomorrow.
      \n

      \n\nEero_Tuovinen \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n

      \n
      \n That's pretty much the epitome of a matter of taste, elevating incoherent design over coherent. Good for anybody who likes it, I suppose. I mean, declaring that a given game text is an incoherent mess you can read any which way is just about the nastiest thing classic Forge analysis gives as an outcome, so if you know that you like that sort of thing, then there's basically nothing that the Forge devils can do to you or your favourite game. That's about as pure a matter of taste as they come: one guy saying that he likes games to be clear in their purpose and another saying that he likes them to be vague, so as to leave room for the players to develop their own interpretation.

      (I lie, of course there are worse things that games have been called in Forge discourse. Brain-damaging, for one. How many games that's been applied to and how many people agree with it is something else, of course.)

      Of course the real flame wars used to be around people not wanting to accept that their favourite game is "incoherent". I don't know, maybe it's a shame thing of some sort. Can't stand thinking that there's somebody out there who doesn't like your favourite game or something. One would think that accepting that there's a school of thought that prefers something else would be easier. What's it to you if what you might call a "toolbox" is "incoherent" to somebody else...

      I'd think yes, it's possible to write it deliberately. And it's just a small step from there to accidentally.

      I think Mike's observations about the culture into which Vampire arrived make a strong case for why better wording was vital. I mean, we all agree that better wording would have been nice. But in a world of railroady CoC GMs, your wording can't just augment their existing habits with a few pointers here and there; you need to actively combat their bad habits. Why? Because these bad habits could ruin their game, and the writers of Vampire should have known that. I mean, the White Wolf guys did play RPGs in the '80s too, right?

      Or maybe I'm setting the bar too high, and CoC was in fact the standard White Wolf was aiming for. Both CoC and Vampire seem to have similar histories of fervent fandom, much awesome play, much awful play, some amazing modules, and some awful modules. If Rein-Hagen was in one of the CoC groups that had an awesome time, maybe he didn't think more was needed. I dunno, still seems like shitty market research at the least.
      \nHere's the thing though David: Both of these games (CoC, VtM) seemed to get a lot of their fans with good experiences coming from gamers who'd either had bad experiences with D&D or just wanted something different from D&D. I think it would be worth considering that aspect of how game culture of the time fits in too.

      We might think of these as coming from a "Please God, anything but more gamism!" urge, and very early forays into trying to follow up on that urge.

      \n Thinking back to my own experience, when Vampire (and later Werewolf, and Mage...) hit my gaming club (and it was a big hit - Bradford, UK in the early 1990s was chock full of goth roleplayers) we were definitely playing it wrong, even by the inconsistent/unread instructions in the books. We'd play with 7-8 players and a GM, with maybe 2 or 3 players coming in every week and the rest having various levels of attendance. From what I recall, our adventures were an alternating pattern of Cthulhuesque breadcrumb-following (often leading to exasperated roadblocks where we'd sit around and wait for our enemies to do something bad so we could try and pick them up again) and AD&D-style 'dungeon'crawling where we'd rock through various nightclubs, mansions, sewers etc. fighting other supernatural (or not) enemies.

      I remember playing a little, but the games were always dominated by one or two players and I can remember most of us would sit around for ages waiting for a fight so we could do something, with very little engagement in the game. I don't think Mark Rein dot Hagen (or indeed any more successful Vampire developer) would've had to stay very long in our sessions to identify a few of our problems.

      But what this discussion is saying to me is that I should crack out Vampire and give it another shot, using all my newly-formed bad/good habits from storygaming. Maybe we should all just hit up IRC/G+ and get our Vampire on for a few hours....

      \n Millsy:
      That actually sounds pretty familiar. Players of RPGs generally when I was playing all had some D&D experience in their backgrounds, and we tended to have a lot of fighty-fighty on a regular basis, and actually very little in the way of angstiness or really much else. I also saw a good bit of semi-Cthulhu style mystery following ( but that would have been fairly common with the folks who had some Cyberpunk or Shadowrun background too).

      I know that nasty incoherence word has come up in this converstaion, but I have questioned before how much of that incoherence wasn't in the game text ( although I agree it could have been better written, along the lines of what David Berg suggested above) but was simply the clash between the common playstyles of RPGs generally and what VtM was at least trying to get in as a playstyle ( even while using fighty/adventure style mechanics).

      Really, for me, the clash with VtM was one between the folks coming from a Genre Emulation: VampAngstGothicism Right To Dream mindset ( and I'm guessing these are the folks who ended up dropping a whole lot of mechanics on a regular basis but lurved the fluff) and the Superheroes with Fangs folks, largely coming from a semi-gamist mindset.

      Am I doing it right?

      Since I was apparently playing games using story game approaches back in the day\u2014using improv teachings I was learning as a community-theater boy in actual play of Vampire (2nd) and Werewolf and Wraith, for example\u2014I can say with confidence that those games could participate in what we might otherwise call very modern styles of play. (I'm something of a hipster about this and I hate the position it puts me in.) And I didn't come to Vampire on any escape trajectory; I played D&D in a fairly collaborative, improv, freeform manner, too. I didn't realize I was doing it "wrong." I thought I was having fun.

      I feel like there's an impulse to lay blame at the feet of games like Vampire for messing up someone's experience somewhere or doing damage to the course of the hobby or whatever. That strikes me as hazy and pejorative. First, I learned a lot about play from those books and those games, so I'm evidence that reading them isn't necessarily confounding. I'll also offer testimony that you can play those games in the style that we attribute to story games (but which, IME, was around in the 1990s), even if, yes, some GM and player principles may have to be imported. I did a lot of what we'd call "hacking" back then.

      Second, what's a rebellion without elders to rebel against? I doubt modern games\u2014even if they're made or played in spite of the previous generation of games\u2014would exist as they do if not for the course of history. Even if you want to regard the '90s storytelling style as a dead end offshoot (I don't), I don't get why it's so alarming or galling or whatever. It's a thing. It happened. Let's learn, look forward, and play, right?

      I mean, how dare propeller planes get in the way of the invention of the jet engine? They should have just designed planes better back then.

      \n Okay, that was fun, now I'll quit fooling around and get back to it.

      One question asked earlier was "what exactly is the role of the rules". Very cogent question, considering the Storyteller makes the framework and the consequences and the players make the plot decisions. The only thing we've seen so far is that the rules "direct" things.

      Some people in this thread have pointed out that by this, you could play the game completely diceless. And indeed, much of the game is set up to be that way. The automatic success rule can keep a lot of play going without touching the dice, and "Complications" like the combat system don't need to be used.

      So let's talk about the rules' role in Vampire play. After a short intro paragraph (addressed to the Storyteller), we get a firm re-establishment of the toolkit nature of the game:\n
      It is up to you to present the rules to the players in such a way that they easily understand how they function, yet are able to use them flexibly enough to provide for realistic and dramatic scenes and encounters.

      As a Storyteller, you will quickly come to develop your own personal style. Part of this will arise from your own personality and inclinations, and part of it will reflect the tastes of your players. The range of different styles is best illustrated by reference to two archetypes, representing the opposite ends of the scale. Both are exaggerated, and you will probably end up somewhere in between.

      At one end of the scale is the Rules Lawyer. This type of Storyteller insists on dice rolls for everything, and applies the letter of the rules to the fullest extent. If a character is sitting on
      a chair, the Rules Lawyer might ask the player to roll Dexterity to avoid falling off. There is a knock at the door, roll Perception to see if you hear it. The character rises to answer the door, but must first roll Dexterity to stand up successfully, Perception to find the door, and Dexterity again to get it open. Ridiculous, of course, but it demonstrates how extreme it can get.

      At the other end of the scale is the Freeformer. To this type of Storyteller, the story is everything. Dice are rolled only occasionally, and then only for the nice sound they make. In fact, the Storyteller decides without dice exactly what is going to happen, and is guiding the players through it just as though they were reading a book. Character actions may direct the story, but it is the Storyteller who decides the results of the actions.

      Vampire as a game tends to be more free form than rules-oriented. The rules are there to help, not to govern, but the players should have the maximum freedom of action, and never feel that their decisions and actions do not make a difference.
      \nEmphasis in original. I think this may be the first time a word has been emphasized in any of the sections I've seen.

      So this section basically describes a scale, with both extremes being bad, and says, the game tends towards the "free form" side of the scale. So our previous guesses/analyses were spot on. (Interesting that the text seems to also think that the rules help prevent railroading!)

      Worse than why, HOW? Like, the game literally says don't railroad, in many, many different ways, and sets up mechanics to help stop railroading (Disciplines, Backgrounds, etc.) So other than better wording, what else is there?
      \nIt also says to railroad in many different ways. In the text you've posted.

      A selective reading of the text could allow you to extract a methodology opposed to railroading. Another reading of the text could easily allow you to extract a methodology supporting illusionism and railroading.

      I don't necessarily find either of these methods shocking or abhorrent, but I think you're exaggerating a bit to suggest that this game is stridently against the play-style it has so often appeared to promote.

      In addition, special powers and backgrounds do not prevent railroading. That's a non-sequitur. Railroading being an out-of-universe meta-diegetic pattern, the two things you mentioned are in-universe diegetic elements. Diegetic elements cannot solve meta-diegetic problems.

      So I guess my big question is, given that Vampire experienced a great deal of dysfunctional play, why did it do so? If the books unambiguously promote positive play-habits (a fact I do not believe) were people just ignoring the books?

      If so, why? Cargo-cult players like myself must have been doing something very wrong to take a clear and functional text and produce the many examples of terrible play that we did.

      What were we doing?

      \n And now...the infamous Rule Zero!!!!! (I couldn't resist the exclamation points, sorry sorry.) Not named that of course. But let's see what the game advises about when to ignore the rules.\n
      One of the biggest decisions a Storyteller will ever make comes when he first decides to ignore the rules. This is perfectly legitimate, provided it's done for the right reasons.

      From time to time, situations will arise where applying the full letter of the rules would cause the entire story to collapse.

      For example, the characters have just spent several weeks finding and recovering an ancient tome, which will give them vital information about the Sabbat who have entered the city. Without the information in this tome, the characters will stand no chance at all of surviving an encounter with the Black Hand, who have become alerted to the characters' existence and
      intentions, and will react soon.

      A freak botch sends the character carrying the tome falling from a rooftop into a huge fire. It is the result of a mortal Vampire-hunter trying to destroy the characters, though he knows nothing of their mission. The tome is lost forever, the characters are doomed to die at the hands of the Sabbat, the city cannot be saved, and the whole Chronicle comes to an end.

      As well as being a crushing and probably undeserved defeat for the characters, this single bad roll means that the story cannot proceed. That can be very frustrating if you've spent your hard-earned cash on buying it or devoted several weeks of spare time to designing it. A couple different repairs are possible, though, so don't despair.

      One is to discount the botch - reduce its severity so that the book is saved, or simply have the player repeat the roll. This works fine, but you may find players relying on this kind of aid from you every time they get themselves into trouble. Best not to get them into bad habits if you can avoid it.

      Another option is to amend the story. The book is lost, but somewhere there is another source of information which can replace it. Perhaps one of the Sabbat is sickened by the excesses of his fellows, or moved by ambition or revenge to turn against them. He seeks out the characters and arms them with the knowledge they need. Again, this will work, but you may find players blatantly sitting around, waiting for you to spoon-feed them in future situations.

      Thirdly, you might allow the falling character a superhuman (or super-Vampiric) effort to throw the book to one of his comrades so that the story can continue. This is probably the best option for most Chronicles, but it may not work for you.

      There are other ways out of this situation as well; your job, and privilege, is to decide which means you will use.
      \nTwo interesting things about this:

      1- Ignoring the rules is done in this example to PROTECT player characters and their efforts from being useless, and preserve the chain of player action-Storyteller consequence. Particularly note how the Sabbat are described. They are "poised to react". This reinforces what the Storyteller's job is.

      2 - Note that the defeat due to the botch is considered "undeserved". The players may not deserve to win but they don't deserve to lose because of one roll. Remember the "Don't" above about luck?

      3 - The preferred option is NOT what-people-would-call illusionism (bah, it's all participationism, jerks), it's just extending the situation a bit.

      I know that's three, but I'm a heavy tipper.

      \n Not sure what you're referring to there, Deliv. And sorry, Jack.

      Anyway, as mentioned, most games of this era, or any era, give very little attention to failure states. I've scoured the book and I can only find a couple of little paragraphs, in the Storyteller section:\n
      In the excitement of the game, especially when things are getting tense, there is a possibility for even the most mild\xad mannered of players to become agitated, start shouting and perhaps drown out the others. You may find that players with strong personalities are dominating the quieter ones, and in extreme cases the loudest player in the group may end up running the entire party of characters. This, obviously, is un\xaddesirable, and players who feel themselves to be overshadowed or ignored will become bored or resentful, and won't enjoy things as much as they should.

      Take care to listen to all of your players equally. If one player states that another player's character is doing something, check with that character's player first. 1f one or more players are dominating the action and not letting the others participate as fully as their characters deserve, take them aside and point out the difficulty as diplomatically as possible. Ask everyone to co\xadoperate and remind them that this is not a competitive game, and those players who seek to dominate others are gaining nothing but unpopularity. As the final sanction, you may decide not to invite an unrepentantly troublesome player to future games.
      \n

      \n There's a 2-page section on storytelling techniques, including description (long flowery descriptions), imagination (don't always use long flowery descriptions), timing, changes of pace, deadlines, delaying success, secrecy, intrigue, invading their "safe haven", scope, and dependents. Advanced storytelling techniques include flashbacks, parallel stories, foreshadowing, dream sequences, and symbolism. Not going to go into a huge amount of detail on these...they're pretty much what you would expect from a game published in 2015.

      There's also two pages of different sorts of conflict that you might put at the core of your stories (this also includes ANOTHER bit on why you shouldn't railroad and how to avoid it, similar to what's gone before...what the hell, I'll put it in):\n
      The best way to be ready for what the characters are going to do next is to simply have a fum and detailed grasp of the setting. There are a number of Vampire supplements which you can use to gain an even more detailed understanding of the world, but your efforts are essential to give stories the depth they truly require.

      Though the players may go and do what they want, it doesn't mean you don't do any work. On the contrary, it simply means you have to work all the harder to come up with appro\xadpriate things to interest them - such things as characters, plots twists, traps and obscure items.
      \nAnd in the theme section, here's an antidote to the "but it promised SOOO MUCH..."\n
      The story becomes larger than life when you make it not only an adventure but a moral lesson as well. Faerie tales, myths and even Star Trek\u2122 use this technique to make stories more vivid and compelling. This gets at why we tell stories in the first place. Stories almost always have a moral. However, in good stories, the lesson that is learned changes from listener to listener. The Storyteller shouldn't have a prepackaged lesson ready; rather, it should present a situation and let the players make of it what they will.

      The theme is not meant to make the story more serious or high-browed. Neither is it intended to give you the opportu\xadnity to diatribe on right and wrong or good and evil. The theme is simply intended to help you create a better story, a story with more depth, pathos and significance. Don't put too large an onus on the theme and it will work out for you.
      \nYes, in this game we might create a story as "true and terrible", as a previous poster put it, as a Star Trek (tm) episode!!! But don't count on it, it's not that high-browed or serious.

      \n And in the plot twist section, just in case there was any doubt:\n
      Climax: This is the great resolution of the story, where all questions are answered (or at least some). If the characters are successful , and they should not always be, then this ending must satisfy the players. In any case, it must be a logical result of events that you set into motion at the start of the story. The climax cannot be forced - the players must be emotionally prepared for your ending. It must descend naturally from what has occurred before and be affected by what the characters have done. The characters' actions must have an effect upon the nature of the story's conclusion. The climax should never be preordained.

      \n Okay, remember the hierarchy:

      Chronicle is what the Storyteller designs and aggressively controls at the beginning.

      Story is what the Storyteller designs more collaboratively and with the characters' situations and abilities in mind.

      Plot is what the characters do and the Storyteller reacts to.

      I've found the Chronicle Concept section and talked about that, now here's the Story section where Storytellers are given story concepts to choose between when working on that level of things. It's a lot more general and question-asking than the Chronicle Concept, which makes sense given that it would have to be customized based on what the characters do.

      The concepts explained are: Adventure, The Masquerade, Tragedy, Jyhad, Escape, War of Ages, Intrigue, Vendetta, Mission, Urban Nightmare, Romance, Wilderness Trek and Redemption.

      Here's an example of one of them:\n
      The Masquerade: Neonates' temptations to make the world what they will via their newfound powers must be tempered by a realization of what an aroused world can do to them. Often the players will have to deal with hunters without killing, injuring or even threatening them. At the same time, they must prevent mortals from discovering the truth about the existence of Vampires. For instance, killing the newspaper reporter hot on their trail will only draw the attention of even more journalists, so the players must figure out a way to get him off the trai1.

      Alternatively, some other Vampires may become too obvious for the players to allow their activities to continue unhindered. How do they deal with a rock star who can back up
      his claims to immortality?
      \n(Yes, let's just use an actual Anne Rice plot.)

      Although this is pretty comprehensive, I think we've all agreed an explicit and consistent explanation of the difference between Chronicle, Story and Plot could have been a helpful through-line. This section is no exception.

      \n It wasn't just the depth of the fail in that example that was the problem, it's that it was "unwarranted" and due to bad luck. So that's only partly a contradiction. The player's shouldn't always succeed, but they shouldn't fail due to just bad luck or in a way that is "unwarranted", i.e. that's not connected to their choices and actions.

      Also, the non-competitive nature of the game is based on the PCs needing to be a team (that's why players pushing EACH OTHER around is bad.) It doesn't address PCs trying to win. Not at all a contradiction, not even the slightest. Go back and look at the "winning" section. There's a ton more stuff on teamwork too.

      And "more significance" is not the same as "high-browed". It's a scale, high-browed shit is at one end, nonsense is at the other, using themes gives your game more significance than not using one...you know, like a theme in a Star Trek (tm) episode! (That (tm) will never not be funny.)

      Absolutely none of these things are serious contradictions.

      \n This discussion is reminding me of a thought that does creep into my head on a regular basis: RPGs are a great excuse to play pretend and a permission to do so and a great inspiration for specific flavors of play pretend.

      They really are a bit crap as games, though. Maybe we really ought to stop thinking about them as what they're crap at and start thinking about them in terms of what they're good at.

      Why didn't people ten years ago practice modern medicine? What morons.

      Am I doing it right?

      Since I was apparently playing games using story game approaches back in the day\u2014using improv teachings I was learning as a community-theater boy in actual play of Vampire (2nd) and Werewolf and Wraith, for example\u2014I can say with confidence that those games could participate in what we might otherwise call very modern styles of play. (I'm something of a hipster about this and I hate the position it puts me in.) And I didn't come to Vampire on any escape trajectory; I played D&D in a fairly collaborative, improv, freeform manner, too. I didn't realize I was doing it "wrong." I thought I was having fun.

      I feel like there's an impulse to lay blame at the feet of games like Vampire for messing up someone's experience somewhere or doing damage to the course of the hobby or whatever. That strikes me as hazy and pejorative. First, I learned a lot about play from those books and those games, so I'm evidence that reading them isn't necessarily confounding. I'll also offer testimony that you can play those games in the style that we attribute to story games (but which, IME, was around in the 1990s), even if, yes, some GM and player principles may have to be imported. I did a lot of what we'd call "hacking" back then.

      Second, what's a rebellion without elders to rebel against? I doubt modern games\u2014even if they're made or played in spite of the previous generation of games\u2014would exist as they do if not for the course of history. Even if you want to regard the '90s storytelling style as a dead end offshoot (I don't), I don't get why it's so alarming or galling or whatever. It's a thing. It happened. Let's learn, look forward, and play, right?

      I mean, how dare propeller planes get in the way of the invention of the jet engine? They should have just designed planes better back then.

      (Edit: Someone told me to be more provocative online. Is this that?)
      \nWill, rather than return snark for snark, I'll just say that you seem to be mistaking the opposition position of "I don't think Classic Game X, solely by virtue of its own text, supported [S-G APPROVED PLAYSTYLE] more than it hindered it" for "ha, ha, old games are 100% shit", which I don't think a single person in this thread has said except for you or Jason "jokingly" accusing someone of doing it. And especially no one has ever claimed [S-G APPROVED PLAYSTYLE] didn't exist in the '90s. The whole issue is over how easy or hard it was to get at [S-G APPROVED PLAYSTYLE] using the tools the book provided, in the language that it did.

      (Which is probably an impossible argument to resolve since it is completely anecdotal, but everyone likes a good debate)\n

      \n Yes, that's a contradiction (rolling dice in a game where luck is discounted), I think it's fairly well explained by the preceding 'what the rules are for' section, where the idea that the Storyteller should just make up what happens in free-form style is disapproved.

      I agree that if they ended up on the rooftop because of their own choices and the natural outcomes rather than by chance or Storyteller scene-setting that the example would go differently! As I said, we can imagine all kinds of contexts for that example that mean different things. I don't think the example proves much of anything except that the game is trying to emphasize decision and consequence and sometimes fluke die rolls will get in the way of that.

      Like, I don't think the example really has a context in which getting up on the roof is the question. They're on the roof, we assume due to normal but somewhat blinkered play, uh oh, a fluke roll means the game might end up being dissatisfying and un-fun for everyone if we don't do something about it. Don't throw your game off a cliff, instead, do something else. Of course if you can prevent the example from occurring at all, the book clearly counts that as a win!

      All right, on to other examples. People who say "oh, this book doesn't SHOW you how to play" are also drunk, because that example is one of many, MANY examples in the book. As I mentioned, even the art is an example of a plotline involving a vampire lady who ends up obsessed with this vampire dude and then Embraces a guy who look like him in the modern age and he kills her. It's pretty neat.

      Here's some more:

      There's four pages of an "Example of Play" which is written as a fiction on the left hand column and with game mechanics on the right hand column. Here's an example paragraph from each:
      \n
      The only access to the brewery is through a rooftop doorway. There is no easy way up the brewery building itself, but a restaurant next door, Daley's, has a fire escape and the jump to the brewery rooftop isn't that tough, especially for a Vampire. Mark decides that it is of moderate difficulty (a 6 or better needed on a ten-sided die) to jump from the roof of Daley's to the brewery. Elucid is the first character to arrive at the brewery. After climbing the fire escape, he rolls his Strength + Athletics to see if he makes it across the gap to the roof of the brewery. Elucid has a Strength of 4 and an Athletics of 2. He therefore picks up 6 (4+2) ten-sided die. This is his Dice Pool. Elucid needs to get at least one six or better on his dice to succeed in jumping to the roof of the brewery. He rolls and gets a 9,7,7.4,2,1. Stewart has rolled 3 dice of seven or better for Elucid, but he has also rolled a I, which cancels out one of his successes, leaving him with 2 successes. Therefore, Elucid leaps across the gap and lands safely on the roof. The Storyteller has Malcolm and Travis make the same action when they arrive and both succeed, though Malcolm only has one success and for a second, almost falls over the edge before he regains his balance.
      \n
      With superhuman grace, Elucid leapt the distance from the roof of Daley's to the brewery. Word had it that the only entrance was through the brewery's roof Sure enough, Elucid could make out the forms of various other Kindred as they descended down the stairway to his left. A young black boy shoved his way past, jostling Elucid into another Kindred, a rugged man with a wild, drugged look in his eyes. The two of them stared at Elucid openly, and gave him wide berth. He was used to that.
      \nIt's cool, and mood-setting and stuff, but it didn't really add much to understanding the game past providing more examples for how and when to roll stuff, which was already done (as I mentioned) extensively in the character creation and system sections.

      \n\nDeliverator \n\n\n

      \n It seems to me from these quotes that, while there is indeed some good stuff in there, all of it is mixed in, often in the same paragraph, with utter trash that obviates that good stuff.

      So yeah, I'd say VtM is 100% shit as a product. Doesn't mean people couldn't have fun with it. It seems that one of a couple options had to pertain in order for a group to get functional play out of it:

      1) JDC's approach: come to it from a background of even worse stuff, and cherry-pick the anti-railroading text over the pro-railroading text because of that (understandable) bias.

      2) Will's approach: bring techniques and tools from improv and education and invent modern S-G techniques or something quite like it and use them in place of or on top of the official rules.

      3) Use an essentially free-form approach with a group that is okay with ignoring the dice and all the numerical, mechanical rules in the book and simply riffing off the kewl flavor.

      Note that all of these require\u2014flat-out require\u2014doing a combination of a) ignoring large chunks of the text and/or b) adding your own ideas to what is there.

      Any game where functional play requires that much modification is both bad as a piece of RPG writing and harmful to the social cohesion of any local play group that tries to use it RAW.

      Any RPG should be able to be played 100% by the book and be fun. No RPG text should contradict itself every couple of pages. That's not too much to ask, even in 1991. Such games did exist then; even 2E D&D was much, much more coherent at its core*.

      Matt

      *2E's problems are myriad, but they're basically all math issues, and not a lack of clarity on what the players of the game are supposed to do or how the game is supposed to work.

      as if you are to use the rules, but you mustn't use them, but you must, but you mustn't...the swirling contradiction.
      \nYou have just successfully described most of human existence in most societies during most of history.


      I don't know, I'm reading the same excerpts you are, and while I can see that there are contradictions, I can also see that the text is trying to convey a rationale for threading your way through those contradictions to get a better game experience. While I think we can agree that it falls short of the ideal, it's at least making a laudable attempt to teach what the authors felt were Vampire's best practices, and coming back to it multiple times in different ways in case one of them might click with the reader more than the others.

      I didn't come into Vampire until 2nd edition, but from what I remember, I understood the "don't let one botch ruin the game" pretty clearly at the time. I and the people I played with were all in agreement that it sucked when a game ended in abrupt, abject failure for everyone because one person got one low roll. We'd been through the AD&D meatgrinders, and all it taught us was that you should never get invested in your PC or the plot, because the odds were against you ever seeing a satisfying ending for either one. Vampire was saying, no, you should care about your characters and about the overall story, and in the worst case scenario where a freak botch could destroy them, rather than shrugging and saying "Welp, I guess that's game over," the GM should consider ways to let the game (the characters and what they're doing) continue on. And furthermore, that there were bad ways to do this and better ways to do this, and the GM should aim for "better" whenever possible.

      I wouldn't go back and play 1e or even 2e if you paid me (well, unless you're a crazy millionaire or something; my price isn't cheap, but I definitely have one), but what I'm reading here is reminding me of all the good things I learned from Vampire. It wasn't a perfect game text, but it was a good one that taught some valuable things, contradictions and all.

      \nNot...really? Like, that's not what the rule zero text says?\n
      I've basically given two options of either it's their own choice, or they were railroaded.

      Are you saying there's a third option? Otherwise such 'scene setting' is just regular old railroading.
      \nWait...you don't think there's any other thing that happens in games with GMs other than player choice and railroading?

      ?!?!??

      This seems like burying the lede. Can you expand on this, maybe in its own thread?\n
      I think if any progress is to be made on this subject of conversation, it'd probably be granting that the book fails, to some degree, in it's goal. And it's okay to play a game that fails its goal, it's fine.
      \n100 percent of all things on this fallen earth are imperfect and miss the mark. I completely agree.\n
      Like, I don't think the example really has a context in which getting up on the roof is the question. They're on the roof, we assume due to normal but somewhat blinkered play, uh oh, a fluke roll means the game might end up being dissatisfying and un-fun for everyone if we don't do something about it. Don't throw your game off a cliff
      \n
      Here's the assignation of responsibility - was the GM breaking the rules when he called for that check?
      \nNo? Maybe he just made a mistake, or didn't quite get what was going on, or was....(gasp)...A NORMAL HUMAN BEING?!?!?!?!>?\n
      If not, then who is throwing the game off a cliff?
      \nNo one, the game doesn't want you to do that.

      Wait, is the question about an imaginary counterfactual where the GM says "you fall into the flames" (rolls damage) "the book is destroyed, and you burn to death" (adjudicates Sabbat attack fairly and without remorse, playing out the consequences of player decisions fairly and completely) "the rest of you are all killed, the end" and everyone (including the GM) is disappointed and grouchy that a bad die roll ended up ruining the game? And then...you're asking whose fault it is this game was ruined? Seems pretty obvious to me it's the GM, who is ignoring the suggestion in the Vampire 1ed. book to not let a die roll take a game down a blocked path.

      Or maybe the designer, who didn't make that suggestion strong enough?

      Or maybe the players, who didn't understand the system well enough and got themselves in a bind due to their own boneheaded decisions?

      Blame is not what the example is about, I don't think I can answer.\n
      I think you're treating extrapolation as being the same as being shown. Unless all vampire play is is jumping from from/to brewery roofs and nothing else.
      \nWhat in the hell?!?

      Did you just say that examples don't show how to play??!

      What are they there for then? I mean, here they're obviously put in place to intentionally hurt people, but in other games, why are examples of play there?\n
      It's cool, and mood-setting and stuff, but it didn't really add much to understanding the game past providing more examples for how and when to roll stuff
      \n
      When to roll stuff. Does this 'when' seem tied into the idea of story to you?

      Apart from the books telling you its a story telling game, how does this roll for jumping across a roof tie into story. Indeed, as Deliverator asks, what would happen if they failed?
      \nIn this example perhaps more than any other. In fact, I think that's the only really great thing about this example. It literally puts the roll in the context of an overblown purple-prose Vampire story.

      And as for "what would happen if they failed", It's a silly question since I'm not the Storyteller and you aren't the players? I mean, I don't know what would happen if they failed...they would get injured, do you mean? Or maybe they would get in another way, or find some other method of getting the information? Or if the GM has made a mistake in setting things up this way he fixes his mistake somehow? I don't even understand how you expect that any human being on the planet could answer this question, how any text on the planet could answer it.

      So let's say in a parenthesis here it says "(The Storyteller knows if he doesn't make the jump he will be rolling X levels of damage, and may have to trade favors with Bob in order to find out what happened inside.)"

      Then you leap on a chair and scream "Ah HA, what happens if the character doesn't persuade Bob to trade favors!?!"

      And then I unveil the footote where it says "If the character doesn't persuade Bob to trade favors for the intel, they might gain it in some other way, through their backgrounds. Be creative!"

      And then you leap from the chair to the table and bellow "And what if the character put all their Backgroud points in Generation and doesn't have any that would apply to gathering intel!!!!!!!!!"

      And then I flip confusedly to the back of the book and in Appendix L discover "If the characters don't have Backgrounds that apply, then the next time they're dealing with a Vampire that does have the backgrounds, have that Vampire scoff at their ignorance of what happened and give them a thumbnail outline. Be sure to shade it in favor of that Vampire's motivations!"

      And then you leap from the table and swing on the ceiling fan, howling "What if there are no other Vampires in the Chronicle!!!!??!"

      I don't know, I don't even understand why this is a question.
      \n

      If so many people was able to play the game and enjoy it without a hitch for 20 years straight, maybe the problem is not in the game.
      \nWell, see, that's the real issue. With game text that contradictory what does "play the game" even mean? Obviously everyone was playing a rather drawn out interpretation of what they thought it meant, or relying on unrelated tools and methodology. Some more successfully than others. The 'game' was successful due to presenting some very cool and sexy style and because of what the text promised, but despite what the text actually delivered.

    How to play Vampire: the Masquerade, First Edition

      If so many people was able to play the game and enjoy it without a hitch for 20 years straight, maybe the problem is not in the game.
      \nWell, see, that's the real issue. With game text that contradictory what does "play the game" even mean? Obviously everyone was playing a rather drawn out interpretation of what they thought it meant, or relying on unrelated tools and methodology. Some more successfully than others. The 'game' was successful due to presenting some very cool and sexy style and because of what the text promised, but despite what the text actually delivered.

      \nI completely disagree.

      Most Vampire games I've seen share most elements, and the ones they don't can be just attributed to the differences between players and GMs, just like with any other game, as DitV, Sorcerer, BW, Universalis, DRYH or whatever.

      The game promised you the chance to roleplay a vampire trying to not lose his shit in a bizantine society filled with treachery, and with many threats (both mortal and supernatural) around you, and that is what you got. Of course you could focus on many different elements, but all Vampire games have Humanity rolls, vampire politics, conflict between sects and all that.

      If you got to play Vampire with a new group, you knew 99% of the time what to expect.

      I'm curious, Jason: does the book have examples that emphasize the freedom, consequences and genuine risks of player action? I mean, if your reading of the game is the reasonable and dominant one, then one would expect that there would be such examples to offset the unfortunately railroad-ish ones, right? I understand that most examples in this style of game-writing tend to be about how to perform task resolution (in itself a neutral issue in this context, I'm sure we agree), but maybe there are others that show how a player makes a momentous decision or the dice come up sideways unexpectedly and how the Storyteller deals with that.
      \nNo examples of in-the-moment rejiggering when a player does something unexpected, no. I agree with you that would have been awesome.

      There is one interesting example of a Storyteller doing Chronicle design, in which he creates a Chronicle with only 7 non-player character vampires, a Prince who pretends he's all that and is really weak, paranoid and ineffectual, and puts the PCs personal lives (and deaths) as the only thing going on in the story that matters politically. In other words, the 7 NPC Vampires, including the Prince, are at most allies with 1 or 2 others. The team of 4-5 PCs is easily more effective.

      From the perspective of someone coming from (say) a Dragonlance module, this is a Storyteller designing a Chronicle and intentionally throwing out many "standard railroading tools" (the uber-powerful NPC, the supernatural power beyond the PC's reach, etc.)

      \nAlmost everything you mention above is setting and color. I have no doubt that most of that was pretty much the same from group to group. None of that tells anybody anything concrete about how to successfully run or play the game.

      Those promises you mention is why the game was so successful. That's some badass and engaging stuff! But a collection of awesome ingredients doesn't tell you how to bake a cake.

      I completely disagree.

      Most Vampire games I've seen share most elements, and the ones they don't can be just attributed to the differences between players and GMs, just like with any other game, as DitV, Sorcerer, BW, Universalis, DRYH or whatever.

      The game promised you the chance to roleplay a vampire trying to not lose his shit in a bizantine society filled with treachery, and with many threats (both mortal and supernatural) around you, and that is what you got. Of course you could focus on many different elements, but all Vampire games have Humanity rolls, vampire politics, conflict between sects and all that.

      If you got to play Vampire with a new group, you knew 99% of the time what to expect.
      \nActually, in my WoD-heavy college days, that was the opposite. In my group and other local ones, there was TREMENDOUS variance on what people thought Vampire was "about". The vampire politics/sectarian stuff was absolutely not a given, and the games I personally played in had none of it; our characters didn't really operate in a context of total cold war within and between sects. Frankly, all the Noddist and apocryphal occult stuff interested us way more.

      (as an aside, I actually skipped the VtM 2 corebook completely somehow-- my first edition was the emo-uber-alles 1st, which I never actually got to play at the time [and I haven't read it probably since then, so I don't remember any of the stuff Corley's quoting; the "reverting to human" illustrated story and all the other Anne Rice touches are the bits that stuck with me], and my college group mostly just read Vampire but actually played the other WW games instead [including Vampire Dark Ages, actually]. By the time we actually gave contemporary VtM a shot, Revised was out so we went with that, and that text pulls back HARD against "Vampire is all about jockeying for control against other undead", so "my" Vampire has never been about that)

      In my experience most of the oWoD was like that-- there were so many incompatible themes and setting elements in every game that pretty much everyone had one specific bit that was their "in", and it was by no means a given that any two people would agree on what made a specific game cool or interesting. Which made for exciting times when a GM would pitch a game no one else wanted in on, or worse, when assumption clash set in after the campaign had started. We had a bunch of games that petered out after two or three sessions.

      Maybe I'm in your unlucky 1%, but all I can do is stand by what I have personally experienced with the game, just like everyone else is doing. I guess what I'm saying is, it's easy to deny that you're in the middle of a minefield if you haven't actually seen someone step on one and get blown up.\n


      Your anecdotical evidence, or Ron Edwards', or any other person's (including myself) means basically jack with a healthy side serving of squat.
      \nYes, you are totally correct. So why do you then follow it up with "I've never ever seen this happen nuh uh (so it is clearly impossible, and the people who say they do on message boards are implicitly lying)" like that does actually prove something? These are not new arguments, in fact I think I have seen Corley fighting this fight on Usenet. That's why this thread is really nice, because we're actually (mostly) sticking to analyzing the actual words in the book instead of accusing one another of being statistical outliers, based on our own sample sizes of one.

      ANECDOTE FIIIIIIIIIIGHT

      \n
      Sounds like a flexible ruleset with lots of options for different modes of play and themes that different groups took advantage of in different ways. That's a feature, not a bug in my book.
      \nYeah, but what happens when division occurs not at the "between groups" level, but the "between players in the same group" level? That is when the feature seems buggier, since the text avoids siding with or against anyone in particular, hindering consensus. This can certainly happen in any game, but some seem more prone to it than others, and as I've said above oWoD seems to invite this kind of failure mode. (and after reading this thread, I strongly suspect it's because getting any meaning out of the books requires such selective reading that it's hard to unsee the sailboat and recognize that other readings may exist. See also Every Mage Flamewar Ever, To This Very Day)

      If one person wants to play balls-to-the-wall coked-out realpolitik, and another wants to play introspective emo weeping, Vampire tells both of them it's the perfect game to play together! Therefore any failure on their part to get along is on them and absolutely not on the book that led them to this conclusion... somehow.

      It is not an insoluble problem, but my group tended to solve it by playing a different game instead.
      \n

      If so many people was able to play the game and enjoy it without a hitch for 20 years straight, maybe the problem is not in the game.
      \nWell, see, that's the real issue. With game text that contradictory what does "play the game" even mean? Obviously everyone was playing a rather drawn out interpretation of what they thought it meant, or relying on unrelated tools and methodology. Some more successfully than others. The 'game' was successful due to presenting some very cool and sexy style and because of what the text promised, but despite what the text actually delivered.

      \nYeah, I mean, it's one thing to take an old rules set and see what happens if you play it strictly by the book, like Luke Crane's recent D&D game. VtM, as has now been confirmed, contains so many paragraphs that contain both "P" and "not P" that I don't even see how it's possible.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\ncreases \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n edited November 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      If one person wants to play balls-to-the-wall coked-out realpolitik, and another wants to play introspective emo weeping, Vampire tells both of them it's the perfect game to play together! Therefore any failure on their part to get along is on them and absolutely not on the book that led them to this conclusion... somehow.
      \nI dunno, I'm not seeing that in the text @JDCorley has been quoting. Correct me if I'm wrong here, but the choice between "balls-to-the-wall coked-out realpolitik" or "introspective emo weeping" is one the Storyteller makes, right? Because those are two different Chronicles. So I don't see the text saying those two players can play this game together, not if you mean one plays the one way and the other plays the other.

      What I'm seeing is, the text tells the Storyteller to make some executive decisions about the premise, theme and tone of the game (that's the Chronicle), and then works together with the players to make sure they create characters that implicate them in an intitial situation (that's the story). The Storyteller is empowered to enforce Chronicle premise, theme, and tone pretty strictly. The game text and rules are supposed to support either kind of Chronicle, but I'm not seeing it promise to support both at the same time.

      So, like, the Storyteller might say, "Okay guys, this time I want some balls-to-the-wall coked-out realpolitik. The setting is Gary, and each of you is going to be implicated in the ongoing effort to get the town out from under the hooks of the Chicago vamps. I want this game to explore some of the tough choices you'll have to make to get free and stay free. Let's just make sure you've got bigger things to worry about than introspective emo weeping. Or at least, more urgent things." That's Chronicle in VtM1 terms, right? And then you start talking about your characters and their pasts, and their relationships to some key NPCs --- that's story in VtM1 terms, and that's more collaborative, right? If one player starts drifting in an introspective emo weepy direction, the Storyteller can say, "Let's reign this in a bit, because that concept doesn't really fit with what I've indicated." And then you Play To Find Out What Happens, and that's plot in VtM1 terms.

      Anyway, that's how I make sense of what I'm seeing in this thread. This thread is reeeeeally interesting from a hermeneutic perspective. It's very cool and curious and weird to see how different people look at the same body of text, and reach squarely opposite readings, even citing the same lines in support of their own view. Vive la diff\xe9rence, I guess?!

      I'm definitely grateful to @JDCorley for going to all this trouble. It's rad as hell.

      \nGiven how you think you can extrapolate my own questions several times ahead and think you are spot on the mark without even asking, it's not surprising you think you know what a few words in a text 'mean' in some kind of procedural way.\n
      So let's say in a parenthesis here it says "(The Storyteller knows if he doesn't make the jump he will be rolling X levels of damage, and may have to trade favors with Bob in order to find out what happened inside.)"

      You still haven't answered the question of how this brewery roof jump ties into story. Even if it's just your own notion of story.

      I think it's because you know it's empty - it confuses hopping from roof to roof with conflict. It's chewing the scenery. And as such it's an example of the point given to you - it really is an example of vampire play. No conflict. Just dice rolls about events for no reason except it seems like you should roll dice at so and so point.

      Perhaps you'll pop up in your chair and say "You can't just expect story to appear everywhere!"?

Whether the game is "beautifully open to interpretation and theme" or "so all over the map reaching consensus is difficult" depends on the observer I guess.

      This is kind of a derail from the main thrust of "is this game responsible for the railroady reputation it has acquired" which the thread seems to mostly be about, but it's an issue that has totally obstructed me getting campaigns together of a game which all the prospective players ostensibly love, that I do think stems from the text. I have a lot of lingering affection for oWoD, but my actual play experiences of it are more honored in the breach, ya know?\n

      \nThat I do totally agree with. Kudos for starting and perpetuating probably the most civil White Wolf appraisal thread I've ever seen.

      \n\ncreases \n\n\n

      \n\nNovember 2012 \n edited November 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      Right, so then the weeper or the politician says "nah, not for me-- I'm out", which seems kind of weird to me considering that both of them profess to love and want to play the book that says "Vampire" on it.
      \nSure, I mean, to me, that seems to speak to a need on the Storyteller's part to come up with a Chronicle that everyone can buy into. Of course, that's a lot to ask. But in this particular respect, Vampire is in no more inherently flawed or dysfunctional than, say, D&D, where you'd still have to make the same kind of choice. I don't see it as a shortcoming of the rules; it's just the way role playing games typically have to be.

      The thing I see VtM1 doing (at least in @JDCorley's quotes in this thread) is, it says something like: The first step in getting a VtM1 game going is to set the agenda for a given game, and that's a thing you do as Storyteller. There are lots of different kinds of stories you can tell about vampires, but you can't play them all: pick one. Here's some ideas. Give the players a premise they can buy into -- get them excited and on board. Then, it's your job to make sure they stick to it. Be strict and crafty about that, because if you don't, the group is very likely to lose focus and drift away from the sweet spot when it comes time for them to take the lead. That's going to happen as soon as character creation is all done (and Preludes too, if you're doing that). That's when your role as Storyteller shifts to adjudicating and reacting, and the players are going to be in charge of what happens next. So make sure you strike the right note loud and clear at the very beginning, so they have the necessary cues for how to do that.

      I'm down with that. It sounds to me like pretty sound advice for any game that doesn't have a very specific premise baked in.

      \n I think I've spotted another failure point, creases drew it out and I was just looking at the example Prelude and yepp, it's there. A lot of times people overlook the importance of the First Session. Some games even have introductory sessions that are different from other sessions to try to get things going in an easy way (Dogs in the Vineyard, Sons of Liberty). But if you're going to do that, you have to end with some energy, some drive to push you into the current situation. (Dogs and Sons both do this very successfully. There was also a "0th level adventure" for new characters to experience in AD&D one time as I recall...)

      Thus, a Prelude in which the character is killed and made into a vampire is Straight Up Wrong for many Chronicles! It's good for Chronicles in which your family or your humanity is what you're trying to protect or regain. It's bad for Chronicles in which you're on a motorcycle and your hair is so good.

      The other thing is that the Prelude is before the story. In other words, it's part of setting up the Chronicle, the time when the Storyteller should/will have the most ridiculous railroading/force power available. So it really is not like normal play. This makes it a BAD introduction to normal play in every way except emotionally. (It is better to do exposition and situation-setting through roleplay rather than through a data-dump, even if the roleplay is forced.) What's worse, is that it's almost completely freeform (it has to be - you're establishing your stats, many of them don't exist yet). So you are not even learning the system or trying out what stats you do have!

      Bah, It's No Good.*

      If I were rewriting this I would say to omit Preludes for players until they've played a few times and are along for the roller-coaster-ride of the Prelude instead of actually trying to explore/learn during the Prelude.

      Here's the most hilarious part from the example Prelude (three pages): Lisa's character Malcolm, a private detective, has just found a gangster's body with the throat ripped out:\n
      Lisa: This is too weird. I'm going to get out while the getting's good. Malcolm is really freaking out, but he doesn't show it of course.

      Mark: Of course. You make your way back to your pad. For the next few weeks, you have the feeling of being watched, but you never can figure out who or what is doing the watching. Finally, after a hard day of work one day, you come back to your pad. While you are removing your weapons, you feel a touch on your shoulder. Whirling around, you come face to face with a ragged looking bum. You recognize him as the low life you threw out of one of those low rent crackhouses you
      cleared out about two months ago. You remember him because, of all the scum inhabiting the crackhouse, he was the only one who went without making a fuss, threatening to sue the city, and
      all that stuff. He had a certain dignity about him - at least for a bum. But as you take this all in, he grabs you by the shoulders.

      Lisa: Well, I try to break his grasp and roll to the right, toward the dresser where my gun is.

      Mark: The bum's grip is like iron and you are suddenly frozen by an almost bestial look in his eyes. Then, like a dream, he starts to rip your throat open with his teeth. Your hands grip the hair on the back of his neck in an anguished ecstasy. (Mark goes into more detail, describing the agony and horror of death). You awake later, with a thirst burning in your throat A quick survey of your $4 alarm clock tells you that it is 9 pm - you must have been out all day! There is blood caked
      on your clothes and hair.

      Lisa: Hang on now, I'm feeling alright?

      Mark: You seem fine except for this burning thirst in your throat.
      \n"Mark goes into more detail, describing the agony and horror of death."

      Sure, I mean, to me, that seems to speak to a need on the Storyteller's part to come up with a Chronicle that everyone can buy into. Of course, that's a lot to ask. But in this particular respect, Vampire is in no more inherently flawed or dysfunctional than, say, D&D, where you'd still have to make the same kind of choice. I don't see it as a shortcoming of the rules; it's just the way role playing games typically have to be.
      \nEhh, I could get behind this in general, but D&D is particular is a really bad counterpick because that game DOES have a really strong premise baked in, in exactly the same way VtM does not. "Be a fantasy adventurer" has a pretty clear line of action, you'll go out and fight monsters, gain all those levels you see XP charts for, probably earn that treasure you saw if you looked in the back of the GM book. WHY you do it and WHERE may vary, and maybe even the HOW, but if you play a game of D&D you've got a pretty clear default action plan. It's right in the name even-- there will very likely be Dungeons and a Dragon, plan for those and you'll probably do okay.

      Whereas Vampire says, you are a vampire. OK, so I go drink blood, maybe turn into a bat, hate the sunlight? And yeah, the game does all that, but that's kind of a thin action plan to work with, so the game adds the politics and the self-loathing and a bunch of other things that don't really play well together. Some things I'd argue that are in direct opposition to each other, and I'm not sure what exactly the book does to help everyone get on the same page besides telling the GM "Make sure everyone's on the same page," with an unspoken "They're not? Too bad." Some people think that diversity is exactly makes VtM so great, that it's multiple games in one. In my experience I've had a problem with people liking one or two of those games and hating the others, and not enough overlap, so we didn't play any of them.

      Maybe spelling out that kind of meta-awareness of choosing flags in character creation more strongly would have helped, as opposed to just presenting the clans, disciplines, etc as a bowl of Chex mix pieces that are implied to all go as great together as Fighter Thief and Magic-User. The "this is a toolbox" rhetoric never went too far with me, because it was all pretty strongly presented, player-side, as one cohesive game; I mean, no one has this problem with GURPS because it actually IS presented as an assortment of tools you use to build a game from scratch. Like, each of the Clans has a style of game it sort of points to (Ventrue or Tremere if you want politics and secrets, Malkavians and Nosferatu if you want to be a boogeyman, etc), but their usefulness as flags is basically never mentioned and the book gives the impression it never even had the idea because the default presentation is that they're all present and actively rubbing elbows with each other. This is a toolbox how? This is a cabin you can rip beams out of if you feel like, more like. Even White Wolf itself had these kinds of internal disagreements about how the game should be played, and occasionally gamelines veered hard away from certain things between editions as developers changed. This is STILL happening if you're paying attention to what's going on with Exalted.

      I definitely, definitely think that more recent designs are more effective in spelling out the failure points of not having a strongly baked-in and actionable default premise than any WW game ever was, and not obfuscating the lack of that kind of premise if they don't have one.

      \n Dark Sun, Ravenloft, Spelljammer, and Planescape were definitely things for me. So, you know, agreeing on campaign setting was a part of negotiating what it meant for us to play D&D.       But in 1991, maybe, VtM was pushing a little bit ahead of the curve. One common vampire mythos, but multiple ways to explore that mythos, telling very different kinds of stories in the same setting. It's no surprise to me that the authors didn't know how to articulate what they were doing, if it was really that novel.

      \n\nAccountingForTaste \n\n\n

      \n
      The "this is a toolbox" rhetoric never went too far with me, because it was all pretty strongly presented, player-side, as one cohesive game; I mean, no one has this problem with GURPS because it actually IS presented as an assortment of tools you use to build a game from scratch. Like, each of the Clans has a style of game it sort of points to (Ventrue or Tremere if you want politics and secrets, Malkavians and Nosferatu if you want to be a boogeyman, etc), but their usefulness as flags is basically never mentioned and the book gives the impression it never even had the idea because the default presentation is that they're all present and actively rubbing elbows with each other. This is a toolbox how? This is a cabin you can rip beams out of if you feel like, more like.
      \nThis is really interesting, and it got me thinking about something I said earlier in the thread:\n
      As an aside, even at the time I don't think I would have ever recommended Vampire to first-time players, and certainly not to first-time GMs. I totally agree with anyone who's said that it doesn't work as a game text when it's separated from the gaming culture of the '90s (and maybe from ANY era, for that matter).
      \nExpanding on this, I'm realizing that when I started playing Vampire, I'd been playing other games for quite a while. A lot of AD&D2e in high school, some Star Wars, some Paranoia, some Call of Cthulhu, some Cyberpunk 2020.

      So when I showed up at my friends' apartment to try out Vampire, I already had a lot of opinions about gaming. I had a nice long list of things I liked about gaming, and a list of things I didn't like about gaming, and even a list of things that I wasn't sure whether I liked or not but definitely wanted to try. The idea of taking a game and skipping right to ripping beams out of it, that was baked into what I might laughingly refer to as my gaming philosophy: of course I'm not going to engage everything in this book, I'm going to be aiming squarely at the things I like and the things I want to try, and actively resisting the stuff I already know I don't like.

      And I don't think that attitude was at all unusual for people back then. Lots of people had opinions, lots of people came from backgrounds where the most natural thing to do was to tailor the game you ran to emphasize the parts you like and ignore the rest, and maybe even explicitly remove the stuff you don't like from the game entirely. Vampire didn't look to me like a big cohesive cabin that needed to be kept intact back then, because no game looked like that to me. It was all parts, and while some parts needed a lot of thought before junking them, other parts were easy to de-emphasize or ditch entirely if they didn't fit the kind of Vampire game we wanted to play. (Scarily, one part of the old Storyteller system just plain didn't work for us and we didn't even try to fix it: combat was a fucking disaster area as far as we were concerned. If you hit, you wouldn't get damage successes, and if you got damage successes, the soak roll would negate them. Every exchange took three rolls and got you precisely nowhere, so fight scenes were just huge pains in the ass. We wanted fast, decisive combat scenes, not draggy ones that made everyone involved feel incompetent and useless. And rather than fix it, I and the people I played with just did everything we could to avoid fights in-character, which I think probably cut us off from doing some of the things we really wanted to do with the game.)


      Anyway, I'm now totally convinced given my own experience and the arguments in this thread that Vampire would be a TERRIBLE choice for first-time players, first-time GMs, and pretty much anyone who was either not comfortable with or just plain not interested in being ruthlessly focused on creating their own specific play experience. If you don't know what you want out of gaming, it's insanely hard to figure out how to get it. If you DID know, or at least had enough opinions to make some solid guesses about what you wanted, it wasn't that hard to get Vampire to deliver all kinds of great, tightly-focused game experiences for you and your friends. The parts are all there and there's plenty of useful advice about how to use them, but it had a very strong assumption that you knew what parts would interest you and that you wanted to make YOUR Vampire chronicle out of them. True for a lot of people at the time (eg, all the people who did enjoy the game), not true for others (eg, the people who did not).

      \n BWG tells you, clearly and concisely and early in the book, with its importance flagged:

      1) What sorts of things to roll for
      2) To set a consequence for failing the roll ahead of time
      3) How to handle it if the character doesn't have the requisite skill

      That particular example you cited may not have had the failure consequence listed, but the book definitely tells the GM to do it, and also how to do it.

      So my question remains: are there any examples in the VtM1 book wherein a PC:
      1) Fails a roll
      2) Other than in combat
      3) The GM doesn't discard or fudge this failed result?

      \n\nDavid_Berg \n\n\n

      \n\nNovember 2012 \n edited November 2012
      \n

      \n
      \n There's this weird thing... you want to jump off a roof... there's no conflict, nothing at stake, you could just as easily not jump off the roof... and the rules say you roll for jumps, and you have a good Agility, so you pick up the dice and are like "Watch me jump, mufuckas!"... but then you roll badly and don't look badass and in fact wind up taking damage... and that's situation fodder! A minute ago you were a healthy guy on a roof. Now you're an injured guy on the ground. Maybe this inspires someone! Maybe the GM has a rival NPC laugh at your plight, or an enemy come to finish you off, or a new ally or love interest take pity on you. Excellent! Good thing we rolled! Let's keep rolling for whatever tasks our stats might apply to!

      But then there are the times when it doesn't go like that. The enemy almost does finish you off, and you're all, "That fun little jump and die roll almost got me killed, fuck that, next time I'm avoiding invoking the dice." Or the GM doesn't have an inspiration out of the blue, so you're just there on the ground feeling dumb, wondering how you failed a jump, blacking out wound levels on your character sheet just so you can erase them before the next day's scene.

      Not a big deal, maybe, depending on your taste and your other RPG skills. But it is a little chaotic. Invoking RPG task resolution often is.

      That's why Burning Wheel's "set a consequence for failing the roll ahead of time" isn't just, y'know, another handy tip like many handy tips in the Vampire book. It's an utter game-changer. I'm not saying that it's in all ways superior, but I think it adds a bit of credence to the idea that asking Vampire to do better might not be so hard or unreasonable. Maybe "establish possible consequences before rolling" or some such. And then tell the player and the GM, "when the roll is failed, here's what the GM is supposed to do," so everyone knows whether the GM is doing it right or not.

      I dunno, JD, does that sound like an improvement to you? Or is that inappropriate to Vampire in some way?

      \n I'm personally not convinced by these various external standards taken to Vampire. It's much too easy to play that game: your favourite game sucks because it doesn't support Radeon 9700. A more useful critique separates what the author attempted (and whether he succeeded) from the generalized question of how useful what he attempted was. Both of these may be discussed, but mixing them up by demanding that a work matches up to your demands is false (and what's worse, arbitrary) entitlement.

      I should note that I'm not sure if any of you fall to the above logic trap, by the way - reading point-by-counterpoint is never easy, at this point I only know that you've proceeded into discussing the ideal length of a Vampire session. It sure seems like this, though, as insofar as I can discern Jason is discussing what Vampire is to his understanding while the loyal opposition is busy proving that not only is Jason wrong, but also what Vampire actually is is sucky. That's two discussions at once, which is unnecessarily confusing: what Vampire is substantially can be discussed separately from whether it would be more worthwhile or could be improved by being something else.

      For my own part I'm pretty inspired by the more substantial parts of the discussion earlier in the thread. I especially like the overall analysis that was floated (I don't care to track by whom, this is probably synthesis anyway), I think this sums Vampire pretty credibly insofar as a historical critique of the text goes: Vampire is substantially a generic rules-set bolted onto an ideal of narrative roleplaying and a setting that is built as a kitchen sink of vampire mythology. Each of these elements (rules, GMing methodology and setting) can be seen and verified pretty easily in the text, and what we know of the general context of the time tells us pretty clearly that it was and is par for the course for these elements to be somewhat disparate, separately designed. Integral game design simply wasn't a thing at the time for various creative and economic reasons.

      What inspires me in this summary is that to truly realize the non-existence of a game (as we now understand the term) in the text of Vampire is to also open the door for taking that setting (the best part of the game, it seems to me) and running with it for your own purposes. It is wrong to say that a disparate design like this is a bad game, for that would be attributing a standard that was not sought or achieved by most designers of the time; rather, insofar as we insist on analyzing the game text with modern tools, it is fairest to say that it is not a game at all. It'll become a game when a GM reads it and determines his exact solution of how the elements align, but at this stage of text it is merely three largely separate things.
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n I'm not so sure, Eero. I think there's a healthy critical middle ground between saying "Vampire was a 1991 RPG, and only 1991 standards can be applied to it" and "every modern gaming standard should apply to Vampire". Presumably, the author's intent, beyond selling books (which was an historic success!), was that people enjoy playing this game. So, discussing circumstances in which people did not enjoy it, and why, and what could be done about that, strikes me as germane. Even if it's only 1% of Vampire groups who stumbled, it's still a thing that happened, that we know something about, and I think it makes for interesting discussion.

      Personally, I don't think that any of the tweaks I've been brainstorming would suddenly apply a whole new set of priorities to Vampire and make it not what it was. But maybe I'm underselling the significance of constraining of "what you might wind up doing with this proto-game toolkit"...? Do you see that as an important part of the author's intent, that individual translation determine the direction of the game so deeply that "failed play" has to be kept on the list of possible directions? It seems to me that it should be possible to provide a set of "do what you want with this!" that is nevertheless a subset of functional play. I'm excited by that possibility, anyway.

      I like trad games, and I like Vampire, and my interest here is in figuring out what might make them easier to enjoy. I think JD's textual excerpting provides an excellent opportunity for that.

      There seems to be some sentiment in this thread that "no game is perfect, everything fails 1% of the time, this is not a problem deserving criticism." I don't think that accurately sums up the situation with Vampire's play history. The world is full of people who don't like football. It's quick and easy to understand what you do in football, and you will never attempt to play and wind up doing something else. Football is what it is, and if it's not for you, it's not for you. This is not a flaw. Vampire (and other proto-game RPGs), on the other hand, convinced some people that "you do X", and then in play, they did not get to do X. To me, that sounds like something that is a flaw. What do you think?
