      \n That's cool, David. I was more commenting on the perception that it's up to Jason to prove that Vampire is a great game, and up to everybody else to prove him wrong. The nitpicking with e.g. Callan seems like the point is to argue about whether Vampire is a good game, which is a confusing tack to take when it hasn't even apparently been established what Vampire exactly is.

      As for Vampire's flawedness, you understand that I'm reserving final judgment until I at least read the text myself. However, from what Jason's been explaining to us here, I get a strong sense of traditional game-text writing, of which I think that I know a thing or two (as do others who were in the hobby in the '90s). Unless somebody actually points out some flaw in Vampire that's more radical than what you've got in any midline trad game from the era, then I'm forced to conclude that the think wrong with Vampire is that it's the most popular of a batch of games that we (as in, most people at this forum) do not like. This is not wrong in context, it's just a reiteration of the fact that rpg design went to the dogs for a time.

      To explain that more concretely, David, consider the manner of evidence we have about the problems of Vampire. People often say that the game is evidently bad because there are many people who recognize in hindsight, or even recognized at the time, that their play was horribly shitty. They never got the game to work. Doesn't this prove that the game is bad? Well, to my mind it doesn't, not when we have alternate explanatory models at hand. We can look at Vampire not as a game but as a discrete collection of gaming tools, the sort that was usually written under the guise of a "game" at the time. This includes a generic rules system that enables some task resolution, a bunch of GMing philosophy that strives to address roleplaying universally (as in, give everybody something and let the GM sort it out) and a flavourful setting. In no way does the presence of these things prove that an unitary game exists in there, and considering the content of this discussion this seems like a reasonable conclusion: nobody here seems to agree about what the game is supposed to be like, exactly, and what we've seen of the book reads to me like exactly the sort of text where the relationship of the mechanics to the method to the setting is all largely incidental.

      This latter notion of Vampire not as a bad game but merely a loosely-written, disparate one, entices me because it is a very good and simple interpretation model for many other traditional games that I know well. For example, Call of Cthulhu is a game that I have read and played (there's a thread out there about my experiences with the game if you want details), and this notion of textual incompleteness fits that like a glove. Thing is, I can't say that CoC is a bad game because it's not a complete game - this judgment doesn't make sense, I might as well blame a Chess book for being a bad Chess because it doesn't include the rules. If you agree that the way traditional game texts are written doesn't describe a complete game without bringing in a GM's own prior experience and creative effort, then there's no way to judge the game as a game, either; it's entirely feasible to say that of all the games that can be gotten out of the book, some are bad and some are good, insofar as game criticism goes.

      What I mean is, talking about Vampire like it was a modern post-Forge game is like shooting at a moving target: you say that something in how Vampire is played is shitty, and then Jason says that that doesn't matter because that's not how Vampire is played. At some points the two of you might agree upon some point that definitely is "Vampire", but then more often than not that bit is completely reasonable - the game Jason sees in that text is completely reasonable, I believe, and the gripe people generally have towards the game is that it's so vague as to enable exactly this sort of interpretationary vagueness.

      Of course even if we are forced to discard game criticism when it comes to games like CoC or Vampire, we're still left with literary tools - maybe these game texts are bad pedagogy or bad literature, even if we cannot judge them as games. Literary criticism is a complex topic, though, and even as I might have some opinions on game texts I know well, Vampire is not one of those. Besides, if a critic tells you that a book you enjoyed is a bad book, how much weight does that carry? And ultimately, it seems to me that criticizing Vampire for features that it shares with nearly all games of its time is like criticizing Don Quixote for having too many side stories in it. That's just how things were then, and there's a reason for why things are different now.

      \n Agreed, there is no one true game delivered by the text for us to critique. What we can critique is the degree of support provided for incorporating the GM's own prior experience and creative effort (among other things) into a fun play experience. The Vampire book is spending many, many pages attempting this. Is the attempt a complete success? No. So, we can ask, how might it be improved?

      We don't have to call it "game critique". Call it "toy manual critique", I guess. Like, a pamphlet that comes with a nerf ball and says "Bright and delicious, don't let anyone touch it," is inferior to a pamphlet that says "Throw it without fear of damage, including indoors, with children, and at close range!"

      \n\nEero_Tuovinen \n\n\n

      \n
      \n Well, yes. For my own part I'll reserve judgment on the details until I read the game myself. Insofar as other viewpoints go, I've phrased out pretty well how the critique here looks to me: many people see the Vampire game text as insufficient, but few contrast this judgment against the historical context of the game. I can't help but think that this is a judgment borne of hindsight. Nothing wrong with this except in that Jason's put into a difficult debating position when his topic is to review and interpret the game while others insist on judging it. Different modalities of discussion, those. Jason's been quite clear about how he's focusing on a constructive reading of the game text (he's telling us how to read the game so it makes sense), so I don't know how useful naysaying is in this context.

      Regarding absolute quality of the Vampire text, you're right, of course: I've yet to see a fully-fledged serious rpg text from the traditional era that was satisfactory about aligning into a complete and clear game in the way a modern text does. I might ironically say that Dragonlance broke the hobby in a way: huge need for artistic expression coupled with staid systemic imagination left game writing trapped in exactly the sort of confusion that Vampire seems to embody.

      In this historical context I'd like to say that the way to speculatively improve Vampire as a game text is the same one I'd take with almost all traditional games: give the author permission and encouragement to write the game they really want instead of a largely conventional text designed to appeal to the largest possible customer base. The focus brought in by taking creative responsibility would pretty much fix the type of pedagogical weakness Vampire seems to suffer. It's a pretty basic job with the benefits of hindsight, really - at this point we've seen so many different games and creative agendas sharply communicated that it's not difficult to pinpoint the exact aims of something like Vampire and communicate them with much more contrast than before. It used to be in the traditional framework that a game could only ever reference "good roleplaying", while today you've got a plurarity of examples of what your game is not like.

      As for what the game behind the text is like in Vampire, I think that Jason makes a pretty good case; the game he sees is something I can believe having been in the mind and on the table of the creator at the time. I'm thinking of the relationship of campaign design, plot and game mechanics, to be specific. I imagine that a hypothetical modernized text would replace the superficial link of task resolution to plot with something more honest, but perhaps most of the game mechanics and such would be preserved; the complex, layered character sheet seems to have a function in this type of game as a way for the player to express his character vision, for example, even if it is not truly key to any of the things the game is really about.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n
      \n
      what Vampire is substantially can be discussed separately from whether it would be more worthwhile or could be improved by being something else.
      \nI think it's pretty obvious what it achieves is being discussed, not just what it is - which is text on paper, nice binding, etc. And if that's being said as a discussion (rather than an advertisment), then there's just one discussion going on.\n
      Jason's been quite clear about how he's focusing on a constructive reading of the game text (he's telling us how to read the game so it makes sense), so I don't know how useful naysaying is in this context.
      \nYou'd say the constructive reading is really being distinguished as seperate from the text?

      \n Callan, I am really at a loss for what you're asking me here. You've asked me for so many extensions and alternate versions of the text that my head's spinning. What if the guy didn't make the jump? Well, in the example, he did make the jump, so is this a question about how examples should be written, or is it a question about how all rolls must have a pre-announced failure state, or...? Like, I don't see what you're trying to get at.

      Especially this stuff about freeform. Vampire says "this isn't a freeform game, but it does tend to be more freeform than rules-heavy". So now you're asking me whether in the rule zero section it disclaims that? No, it really doesn't, it describes freeform and rules adherence as tendencies that can be destructive at the extremes, and says you will probably shift around from time to time. Your sample RPGs don't seem to have any relationship to the game I've been copying and pasting text from for the last five pages.

      Are you saying these sample RPGs would be like the alternate universe RPG in which JDCorley wrote a freeform version of Vampire, or said that freeform was fine? Like...in this alternate universe are we competitors? Is your mini-RPG Nightlife in this other world?

      Can you specifically nail down a question about this text that I can go and answer for you?

      In this historical context I'd like to say that the way to speculatively improve Vampire as a game text is the same one I'd take with almost all traditional games: give the author permission and encouragement to write the game they really want instead of a largely conventional text designed to appeal to the largest possible customer base.
      \nIt has the advantage of focus, and the disadvantage that no one (within the margin of error) will want to buy it!

      Definitely commercial aspirations are written all over this book. There are references to future supplements, and so on, all throughout it.
      \n

      \n

      \n\nEero_Tuovinen \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Yes, you might be right - focus might equate to commercial failure. Times change, though, and even if this is still the case I would hazard that it is much less so; the Internet has basically multiplied the variety of games and other pastimes available to everybody, which makes "sort of sucky but at least everybody can adapt it to their tastes" much less attractive than it was in the 20th century. One might say that what modest success (in comparison to current state of the industry) the variety of focused indie games have had through the last decade has basically been because they're specifically better games than a traditional game that attempts to be generally better. This is going to be a competitive advantage in whatever narrow slice the game's target audience might be.

      But be that as it may, I don't begrudge Vampire its success. It is no worse than the competition to my mind, and I am not the judge of good and bad taste. I would make a different game, but then I work at a different time and for different reasons; a game witten vaguely to capture a larger audience would not occur to me - I wouldn't be the customer of one, so I hardly would create such, either.

      \n I hear what you're saying, Eero, but where I part company from you slightly is on the topic of all that terrible, terrible GMing advice. Even ignoring the game's antecedents and descendants, it's simply bad, contradictory writing, just by the basic rules of English and logic. And it's not stuff you can so easily say belongs in one style of campaign but not another, because of the way the paragraphs are structured. Like, sure, the idea is there that there are different types of Chronicles. But it doesn't say, Hey, you know, if you and your group like to do lots of fighting, you should probably adhere pretty closely to the combat rules, but otherwise just wing it (which would annoy my modern sensibilities but wouldn't be objectionable). It says to sometimes wing it and sometimes not, with very murky advice on when to switch modes.

      Now, I like the idea of labeling it, and most other games of the time, as a proto-RPG. But this then leads to the question, of what we're supposed to understand from the text of Vampire vis-a-vis this thread, specifically its title. It doesn't say "How to maybe play VtM1E" or "JDCorley tells you how to play VtM1E." What are the necessary and sufficient conditions of a) VtM1E and b) how to play it.

      For example, JD keeps insisting that the text tells you not to railroad. OK, but nevertheless many GMs did. Were they not playing VtM1E "correctly"?

      For example, JD keeps insisting that the text tells you not to railroad. OK, but nevertheless many GMs did. Were they not playing VtM1E "correctly"?
      \nDespite the thread title, I've been trying to avoid this one a little because I would feel like the atheist bible salesman - since I don't think there really is a "correct" way to play any RPG - or any game - just rules that we social creatures choose to follow or not follow for our own inscrutable purposes.

      I guess I want to turn this on its head. What would I have to find in VtM1e for you to say GMs that railroaded WEREN'T playing it "correctly"?

      Edit: Or, given that GMs did indeed railroad VtM1e, would this be asking you to discount "your lying eyes"?

      \n\nEero_Tuovinen \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Yeah, the GMing instructions may be of a confused nature. That was often the case with traditional game texts - Vampire surely isn't alone here, AD&D 2nd edition suffers of similar skitsophrenia regarding heroic fantasy ideals vs. heritage rules, just to pick another prominent game from the traditional era. One way to understand these types of texts from the time is to accept that the authors have worked off an unitary view of roleplaying: roleplaying is a single thing, and therefore a game should be all things to all people.

      In this type of textual tradition, then, writers have limitations to how they perceive their topics. Saying "no, this is not part of my game" becomes a hard endeavour. It is achieved not by directly cutting to the heart of the matter, but rather by emphasis and de-emphasis. As Jason quotes, Vampire actually has a surprisingly strident artistic program for the time when it suggests dramatic concerns as clearly primary. It's still a muddle from our viewpoint due to how the game carries heritage content (an adventure game chassis, expectation that players cannot truly be trusted to do more than game their characters, unwillingness to just leave adventure gamers out in the cold in general), but at least the game tries to verbalize something that we can take as its main goal in good faith. I at least am pretty sure from Jason's exegesis that the author truly wanted a dramatic, story-oriented game, which he achieved locally by freeform methodology, and which he tried to write up as well as he could within the constraints of the game text tradition of the time.

      It is not without merit to approach a textual situation like this from the viewpoint of a constructive reading: I could read Vampire objectively to see how it might trip me up when I try to play it, but I could also study the game at the feet of people who dig it and get it, and learn from them how to make the game do what it does best. What I mean is, is the game of the text truly more important than the functional game? If we say that for example 40% of all people who tried to play Vampire interpreted it correctly and got a fun game out of it, then do we want to limit ourselves to the game text when exploring the game? It seems to me that even more important than the game text here is our ability to ask an accomplished Vampirist to explain the game to us.

      This is an approach to game texts that I've become particularly fond of after I started researching D&D from the viewpoint of practice instead of individual game texts. With D&D the game text is quite simply a brazen lie, the reality of the game is in the groups that recombine textual sources into practical engines of roleplaying; the recent headway we've made in understanding and appreciating old school D&D has only been made possible by abandoning the text and listening directly to the people who've themselves made constructive readings of those texts into a cornerstone of actual practice.

      Taking this viewpoint to practice, perhaps we can agree that the game Jason describes to us (whether it's imaginary or not) seems pretty decent - maybe not my cup of tea, but reasonable for the time. We might also agree that disputing the text without reading it carefully ourselves is a waste of time; Jason's actually reading the original text with actual play experience of it to boot, so anybody here who's like me and only has passing knowledge of it shouldn't even be engaging in critical exegesis, we're just here to ask Jason questions. Those who have the text and are familiar with it may be reasonable in engaging Jason's interpretation of the text (in my eyes, I mean - I don't intend to be an Internet police, of course), but even then it should be kept in mind that textual exegesis is difficult, it's not something you can just score points in by engaging in Internet debate. It's entirely reasonable that two well-informed readers will come away with different interpretations of the Vampire text.

      Well, that's a lot of text on the metatext of this text analysis, I'm sure everybody's thoroughly bored by now. I've even forgotten myself what my point was, exactly. I'm pretty sure that it was that maybe we shouldn't harass Jason so hard about incontrovertiably proving his reading of Vampire as the "correct" one - there are no true readings in this postmodern age, there are only useful ones. Just about the worst thing that can happen by not refuting Jason's reading on the spot is that Jason takes a page from the Zak Smith playbook and decides to characterize everybody who didn't get the game right as an idiot. Maybe that's what we're really discussing here: it's very important to convince everybody about Vampire being not just a pretty confusing text, but actually an unintelligible one, for if we do not, then everybody who failed to play the game well is an idiot instead of a victim?
      \n

      I'm curious JDCorley, did 1e have a sample adventure where the vampires go to a party held by Modius, a vampire prince? It was at the back of the 2e book at was a fantastic example of all the principles the rest of the book talked about. And also had some surprising assumptions like you would larp some scenes, and not larp others, which is vastly different from how the culture played out later from my outside perspective.
      \nYes, and another thing cool about it is, although it has the "Prince gives you a task" section, which might lead to Another Railroading Module/Scenario...

      ...the Prince is pathetic, paranoid about nothing, and PCs can find this out.
      ...the task is meaningless, there is nothing keeping the PCs finding this out but opening an ordinary envelope that is literally put in their hands.
      ...there's a clear alternative that has just been shown to be way cooler, the anarch dude who is just all up in the Prince's business.
      ...the Prince can't back up his threats, at all.
      ...the Prince and the other Kindred in town are clearly entirely focused on the PCs and what they're about, since they form a plurality of the Vampires in town in the sample campaign.

      It's pretty great.

      Actually, in my WoD-heavy college days, that was the opposite. In my group and other local ones, there was TREMENDOUS variance on what people thought Vampire was "about". The vampire politics/sectarian stuff was absolutely not a given, and the games I personally played in had none of it; our characters didn't really operate in a context of total cold war within and between sects. Frankly, all the Noddist and apocryphal occult stuff interested us way more.

      (as an aside, I actually skipped the VtM 2 corebook completely somehow-- my first edition was the emo-uber-alles 1st, which I never actually got to play at the time [and I haven't read it probably since then, so I don't remember any of the stuff Corley's quoting; the "reverting to human" illustrated story and all the other Anne Rice touches are the bits that stuck with me], and my college group mostly just read Vampire but actually played the other WW games instead [including Vampire Dark Ages, actually]. By the time we actually gave contemporary VtM a shot, Revised was out so we went with that, and that text pulls back HARD against "Vampire is all about jockeying for control against other undead", so "my" Vampire has never been about that)

      In my experience most of the oWoD was like that-- there were so many incompatible themes and setting elements in every game that pretty much everyone had one specific bit that was their "in", and it was by no means a given that any two people would agree on what made a specific game cool or interesting. Which made for exciting times when a GM would pitch a game no one else wanted in on, or worse, when assumption clash set in after the campaign had started. We had a bunch of games that petered out after two or three sessions.

      Maybe I'm in your unlucky 1%, but all I can do is stand by what I have personally experienced with the game, just like everyone else is doing. I guess what I'm saying is, it's easy to deny that you're in the middle of a minefield if you haven't actually seen someone step on one and get blown up.\n

      If I were rewriting this I would say to omit Preludes for players until they've played a few times and are along for the roller-coaster-ride of the Prelude instead of actually trying to explore/learn during the Prelude.

      \n\nImperator \n\n\n

      \n\nNovember 2012 \n

      \n

      I like designs that give a discussion of what a typical session looks like. D&D4e with their Encounters and other projects give you a sense of how long certain types of session can be expected to run and what activities players can get up to in that time.

      A good Runequest fight ran about an hour so you had a sense of how much time would be devoted to that regardless of how long the total session was.

      Burning Empires has advice on how to run two maneuvers in about 5-6 hours.

      I pulled that figure out of the air. Give people time to show up late, some chit chat, then play to about 10:30. Finish up the beers then go home.

      It might be that the game says you just keep rolling, pausing the action when the end of the night comes, and pick it back up again.

      Advice on how to thread sessions together, how to link sessions into on-going games.

      \nFair enough. It is certainly something that most games could include, though I seem to recall that many games these days tell you that a session usually ranges from 2 to 8 hours, with an average of 3-4. Anyway, more advice is usually a good thing.

      \n This is a pretty fascinating thread. I know I accused Jason of seing what he wanted to see in the Vampire text, but I think the detractors have really shown him up in that regard. Like, I'm in the hater camp and everything, but apparently for different reasons than everybody else.

      See, now I have to admit that when I ran Vampire for my friends, back in the day, I actually ran it exactly the way Jason is saying it says you are supposed to run a function game of Vampire. Really. We got together, I said what kind of game I wanted to run, we talked it over a bit, they made characters that fit the premise, then we played a game where I gave them situations, they said what their characters did, we used the rules mostly as-is, and if I tried to get fancy and it didn't work, we talked it over and worked it out. No railroading, and no dice fudging either.

      I don't remember reading any of the text Jason has quoted here, so I can't say why I ended up running it "properly." I do remember some other people running Vampire who were jerks. But I never thought they were jerks because of the game. The argument that Vampire promised something and then didn't deliver it resonates with me, but not because of the text Jason has quoted (and to be fair I think AD&D 2e did the same thing, at least for me).

      \n Eero:
      That's a great post nailing down something that's been bothering me in the conversation. A whole lot of the discussion really does need to take into account both those heritage mechanics and the play cultures of the time to understand this thing even a little bit.

      Weepers and politicians:
      It's worth noting that, like D&D, VtM had a whole lot of very different inspirations. Just as D&D was influenced by a wide variety of source inspirations and ended up mashing them altogether to eventually lead to it's own sort of fantasy sub-genre, so did VtM. The only real commonality in the VtM fictional insprations is that they all involve the vamps as main characters, not simply monster antagonists. After that, everything else was up for grabs, and they tried to mush it all together. So yes, you could have a player that wanted to play a weepy character ( Louis from Interview with the Vampire) and someone else who wanted to play some sort of secretive aristo ( Baranabus Collins) both saying they loved the game, the same way somebody could want to play Aragorn and somebody else wanting to play Gray Mouser and both loving D&D.

      "Proto RPGs"???
      I may be misunderstanding, but I'd find it pretty odd to call something made in 1991 a proto-RPG, given that D&D is produced in 1974, iirc. Stuff tht came in the decade or so before 1974, or those odd experiments along the way for decades before that, sure, proto-RPGs. But VtM? Nah.

      \n@kobradebob

      I think the proto-rpg thing refers to the fact that, in contrast to a lot of rpg's produced now, older rpg's were "some assembly required". At the table, an average rpg from then and an rpg from now look very similar, but from a textual perspective they are very different. The rpg's then assumed, without saying so, that you would synthesize a game from the elements provided, and from elements acquired from elsewhere (previous groups, other games etc.).

      RPG's now generally don't. If you play Dogs, the Mountain Witch, or Monsterhearts from the text, you get a game out of it.

      You can't really play Vampire or AD&D from the text. There is a peripheral information required to synthesize the steps needed to actually play the game. Vampire has some suggestions on how to do this, observe their advice on the different kinds of Chronicles, but the expectations were very different.

      This is where a lot of my problem with Vampire came from. JDCorley and others describe Vampire as a mutable game where individual groups picked and chose the elements from the admittedly delectable schmorgasboard of options presented in the books. Unfortunately for twelve-year-old-me, the books claimed to be the complete package. They claimed to have all the information and procedures necessary to play Vampire the Right Way. They also made me feel like an idiot when I couldn't figure out what that "Right Way" was.

      By accepting that there were multiple viable ways of playing the game, that it was OK and expected that I cut, change, or emphasize things differently for my group, I think I could have easily had more success.

      \n Personally, as a roleplayer, the creatively exciting thing about Vampire is exactly that it attempts to be a "generic vampire rpg". This is such an insane literary prospectus that I've no choice but to admire it - although I do recognize that the creators might not have seen the project as so ambitious and weird as it seems to me. To my eyes, though, as a long-time fan of horror fiction, a roleplaying game with the concept of "anything and everything vampire" seems crazy simply because it elevates an incidental symbol in the place of central subject matter. It's like if D&D consisted of intricate treatises on the history and sociology of dragons in a mad attempt to be inclusive of every dragon myth ever. Sane as a fox, this idea.

      And Vampire does fulfill that particular promise in spades, that setting is really a clever thing that matches the interactive needs of a roleplaying game to the desired vampire wall-mart beautifully. The clans, for instance, and how they enable the author to incorporate basically all vampire myths ever into one game without elevating or contradicting any of them. The myth of Cain is a very fitting backdrop (I like how aggressively it challenges non-Abrahamic cultures), the dilution of blood, the end-times... it's all very fresh mythology with exact, gameable implications. The setting design does a really good job in not just offering an interpretation of vampirism, but rather attempting to provide a meta-framework for all vampire stories.

      In the light of this overriding vampire-mart game concept it is no wonder that the game doesn't really have a very strong idea of what it's about. It can't be about vampires, as vampires are not actually substantial thematic content by themselves. The best bet is to be about the condition of vampirism as a simile for human condition (addiction, outsiderness, beast vs. man and so on and so forth), but how you gonna make that fly when your game design tools consist of bits and bobs derived second-hand from D&D?

      Interestingly enough the ultimate creative ideal of Vampire ended up being "let's tell dramatic stories with vampires". This is something I probably wouldn't have done myself, had I this idea of making a vampire-mart game; my game would be a horror game about observing the variety of vampiric existence from the outside (human vampire hunters, perhaps), or about vampires trying to figure out their identity, what it means to be a vampire. Instead the primary recommended topic of the game ended up being a sort of soap opera - who trusts who, who loves who and so on. (This is how I've understood the talk about storytelling, anyway - feel free to correct me.)

      The interesting part about this is that insofar as I can discern as a designer, you don't particularly need to care about the characters all being vampires when you're doing soap opera. They could be dragons and the human analogy would be quite similar. It's just drama either way. From this viewpoint it would make perfect sense to play Vampire with any flexible dramatic rules-set. Dust Devils or Primetime Adventures, for instance, could both provide a very robust engine for achieving what the game suggests as the Storyteller's main task: delivering a human drama draped in vampiric clothing. The blood-drinking monster thing seems pretty superficial here, it's mostly an useful crutch that justifies why the lovers can't be happy together or why the protagonist is better than other people or whatever else. It's a flexible storytelling crutch, make no mistake, but making a drama game focusing on vampirism is like making one focusing on alcoholism.

      \n JackFractal:

      I started playing D&D around the age of 12. I've learned in that last few years just how very differntly my idea of what D&D was supposed to play like was from the idea of how it was meant to be played by significantly older players with a different backround and play culture from the one I was involved in.

      Huge, enormous differences. An almost entirely different approach from gamers who were even just a couple of years older than me at the time I started playing.

      As for how different things are now in RPG texts, well you've picked some very niche indie games as examples. I love those games, but bigger, mainstream designs still pretty much follow the same kind of pattern as stuff like D&D ( all editions) and VtM at their core.

      Eero:
      Again, I think you're pretty spot on. I think the original intent of VtM was to produce a vampire-themed soap opera with the occasional action scene, but nobody was quite ready to really make a jump out and away from those heritage mechanics that were considered essential to what an RPG was, sothe designers did basically a frankenstien monster of design. They put recognizable Physics Engine Sim mechanics in, a couple of nods to the struggle with internal humanity (another nod back to the inspirational material), and a whole bunch of non-mechanical advice and encouragement to try to get you to do something other than a vampired-up version of a dungeon crawl.

      \n Yeah, the traditional style/philosophy/tradition of design is still going pretty strong, and it's certainly not going away anytime soon, despite facing stiff competition from video games. (Focused design is in a way a rpg counter-thrust against video gaming that is in the slow process of killing traditional rpgs that rely on GM skills, patience and group chemistry.) I should also note that insofar as I'm concerned there's no hurry for trad gaming to "go away" - I might be frustrated with my own rpg experiences in the '90s, but this is the information age now, and today you pretty much can just blame yourself if you end up playing trad games for a decade like I did before the Forge came around

      I'm sure that we'll be seeing general improvement in the quality of game texts across the board going forward, though. It's inevitable with a splintering culture, all game writers need to up their pedagogical game to maintain their accessibility in a world where you're writing at all age groups and backgrounds at once to hopefully sieve out the few and the bold who'll become your own audience.

      \nTo put it another way, games that rely heavily on a human being to mediate/direct things are not at all in competition with video games, so if you don't want to battle video games, you should have a game that requires GM skills.

      \n I think what Jack is saying is that you can't play Vampire or AD&D2 without a GM bringing some creative material and direction to it. The designers obstinately refused.       \n I personally sure was flailing about in those teenage years, yes. In my first decade of roleplaying perhaps only about half of the game sessions were satisfactory, and about one third I wouldn't even characterize as having achieved meaningful gameplay (although play in a looser sense was of course present). That part of the theory (the Forge theoretical corpus, to be explicit) is the way it is because it matches with experience. A better wedge for critique would be to cast doubts on whether our (as in, the people who experienced this) youthful confusion is relevant to anything. Or one could attempt to explain the confusion and substandard play in some other way - I've seen youth (insufficient life experience), cargo cult situations (no experienced teachers) and general vapidity (we're too stupid to know how to play real roleplaying games) being used as explanations, for example. So it doesn't have to be because the games had something wrong with them, it's just a popular explanation.

      But this is totally a separate question, off-topic. A good one, though. And I do agree with the people who point out that my personal failure to bootstrap a successful rpg culture out of '90s game texts in my childhood is not sufficient proof for claims like "Vampire is impossible to play" and the like. These sorts of eager evaluations are immediately disproven by comparing with the experiences of other people who did not face the same difficulties. I'm sure that everybody would agree that at best we can attempt to explain conditional events like how come a game worked or didn't for some people; trying to somehow prove that Vampire is categorically unworkable is a pretty counterfactual endeavour in a world where a living play culture for the game exists.

      D&D4 and Pathfinder, the two biggest RPGs right now, still need a lot of input/customization to get to the table successfully. Neither of them even have a setting in their corebooks! They have less of a ready-to-play game than Vampire 1ed.
      \nJason, I think you're kind of conflating "this game lacks a predefined setting" and "this game lacks a predefined PREMISE". I think the latter is waaay more troublesome than the former if you're trying to get a game up and running, especially if you're a relatively new GM. "Be a vampire" is in and of itself not a premise with very long legs unless you add some extra context to it.

      As I said before, GURPS is not a game that pretends to be ready to run out of the box without some serious tinkering under the hood, and Vampire does a better job of obfuscating that it's the same way, because of its focus on its fairly detailed default setting. In hindsight, setting a game in "The World of Darkness" isn't much different from setting one "on Earth" in terms of "what do we do now", whereas most games I can think of with a strongly defined setting (including Vampire's contemporaries) do a much better job of implicitly narrowing the premise so that play can actually commence. Greyhawk is not THAT different from the Forgotten Realms in terms of communicating what you're actually expected to go do. I can't think of anyone that's ever played Shadowrun and had serious disagreements of opinion on what the game was "about".

      I'm not going to say Vampire had NO advice on how to deal with this issue, but I also think they kind of tried to have it both ways by having one part of the book look like it was all ready to just jump into and another tucked away in the back saying it wasn't. And in my experience, that lack of clarity was easily passed on to the players, especially players who started with WoD or played it very early in their gaming.

      I don't know if the unlucky % was 1 or 10. No one does. But it stands to reason that, if a game is so vastly played for two decades, that unlucky % cannot be very big. I've seen games peter, and I have been in games that petered. It's not like I don't know what a minefield is But

      1) I am arguing that the petering cannot be the majority of games because the game would have flopped instead of almost outselling D&D and,
      2) Most of the petering I've seen and experienced was better explained by player dinamics than by the game itself. So, they would have had bad experience with most games.

      Seriously, I'm not denyin' anyone their experience, I am not trying to be confrontational for the sake of it, and Vampire is not even my favourite game, but when someone says that one of the most successful RPGs in history is terribad and no one could possibly have fun with it unless they ignored vast chunks of the game, despite all the evidence to the contrary, I have to wonder which kind of theory can make such a poor job at explaining things.
      \nMost of your arguments rest on "I've never seen it happen so I doubt it's important" and "This game sold lots of copies so it must have been doing something right", which are fine opinions but really terrible arguments. For the first, I've seen it happen and seen it plenty so I can't believe it's NOT important, and if sales are inherent proof of merit or artistic success then I guess you've never, ever seen something super popular that you completely abhor?

      This is a dead line of argument that relies on marginalizing opposing opinions, based only on your own. I'm certainly not denying your right to stick up for Vampire, nor am I trying to pretend you didn't have fun with the game or other people did, but just saying so doesn't settle the argument for anyone who disagrees. I could say "Large numbers of Vampire were bought by people who read the books instead of playing them, or to people who played a couple games then quit", with just as much evidence as you've given.

      (please don't start an argument over that, I'm throwing it out as an example of a BAD argument with no way to prove it that only annoys people whose experience contradicts it)

      \n It's impossible to know how my 12-year old self would have taken to the modern games. (Presumably ones intended for children - Dogs is an adult game.) Call of Cthulhu, on the other hand, flubbed bad when I tried to introduce it to some teenagers in 2010 or so. Worse even than when we used to play it 15 years ago. Could be that it's not just the age, perhaps I'm just as bad as a GM nowadays as I was in the '90s. Or worse even, if the little I knew then has grown rusty. All the more reason to stick to easier (or different, whatever) games, I'd say.

      I can't imagine it turning out WORSE than those same 12 year old boys playing VtM as their first (or maybe Werewolf is a better comparison, since that has the same "you are righteous holy warrior correct in your application of violence against the unbelievers" thing), so that doesn't strike me as an argument that it SHOULDN'T be done...
      \nOh agreed. I'm not saying either should or shouldn't be done, just that text is only one of many factors that contribute to an enjoyable play session. I am ridiculing the notion that a game like Vampire should be dismissed as unplayable if you follow the text only. We aren't robots. The whole magic of RPGs is how we as living, complex, contradictory humans ingest the text and rules and synthesize them with the dice and our own crazy personalities to make something exist at the table. And since the text is also designed by humans, it can be "flawed" as well.

      I'm also not saying that you can't critique the text for flaws and inconsistencies.

      It's just this simplistic (and ultimately mythical) coherent text as essential for correct play ideology then being used to a priori argue that Vampire is not even a real game that I am going to have to call bullshit on. I played the shit out of AD&D when I was 11 and we rocked the world. I played both Vampire and Werewolf as a gamer in my 40s (much shorter campaigns and one-shots) and had a total blast as well. How much of this successful playing is due to the text compared to our own human inputs I'm not really sure. Certainly it's a mix, but it was also a mix when we played Dogs and Don't Rest Your Head and Inspectres and honestly we spent more time fretting about the texts of those games than we ever did with Vampire.

      I think your impression that Vampire "is not a game for everyone, but it did its utmost to look like it was the kind of game for a large number of people that it was actually wrong for." is pretty factually inaccurate based on its historical and enduring popularity and reflects the strong bias in the community here.
      \nMy answer to you is the same as I've been saying to Imperator: I see you stating your opinion, not a fact. The fact is that Vampire sold lots of copies-- that does not in any way tell us what percentage of copies were used in successful or satisfying play.

      (and of course, "successful" and "satisfying" are ALSO pretty subjective so it's near impossible to hinge an argument on without spawning a sub-argument)

      Vampire has as many haters as lovers (there are plenty of online communities that tend to dislike VtM) and I don't see why your interpretation of that fact is more truthful than my own. It's "obvious" to you that Vampire works because you've seen it work. It's "obvious" to me that Vampire can fail easily because I have seen it fail easily. And neither of us have good data to extrapolate from so let's not pretend our opinions are anything but.

      (and actually, I have got to take issue with your claim that S-G is significantly biased against Vampire, given how many posters have popped up in this thread to defend VtM or at least say they feel more positively about it due to Corley's analysis, including you yourself)

      I like this thread more than any other WoD thread I can think of because it's mostly talking about the actual text, our impressions of it, and how we've personally seen it used. Let's keep it that way.

      \n I'm not saying that Vampire works or that it doesn't work.

      \n I want to clarify my position.

      I'm not arguing that it's impossible to have a good time playing Vampire, even if all you have is the text and you don't have anyone to teach you or a pre-established play culture or yadda yadda. Nor am I arguing, obviously, that no one ever had a good time playing (their interpretation of) Vampire. My argument, based solely on the quotations directly from the text posted earlier in this thread, is that it is literally impossible to play Vampire based on the entirety of the text because of the contradictory paragraphs that describe both the GM and the PC agendas. Those sections instruct the players to simultaneously do things that are opposites of one another.

      That is, by necessity of basic propositional logic (you can't have both "P" and "not-P"), in order to play a game with Vampire as your inspirational text, one has to make choices about which parts of the book to ignore, and which to include. Not merely in the sense of a toolbox, like using only some of the clans or never using the detailed rules for combat. I'm talking about the description of the jobs of the players and (especially) of the GM being self-negating if interpreted literally and wholly.

      (The thing about how Dogs requires you to make a town is a red herring though. Also irrelevant to the thread.)

      \n Yeah, there are definitely things in Vampire 1ed.'s text that straight don't work: the pricing/availability of Backgrounds in survival-oriented games, the Prelude concept, Difficulty numbers of 2, 9 or 10.

      That doesn't mean that the game doesn't generally work, of course. Vampire 1ed's mechanics work fine for Difficulty numbers between 3 and 8. The Difficulty of most tasks are 5-7, so most of the time, in practice, Vampire's system works fine.

      (And Difficulty 9 is only very slightly wonky, it's likely that you can play with Difficulty 9 die pools every so often and not notice the problem, though it's there. It's especially occulted if you have a Specialty at play in those pools, which you probably will for skills. Not for Disciplines, though, no Specialties in Disciplines in 1ed. (I think))

      Anyway, it's Difficulty 2, which never got rolled, and Difficulty 10, which was rolled very rarely, that are the real dealbreakers for 1ed's mechanics.

      I don't agree there's that much contradiction given the goal of the game to accommodate a great deal of variety among different groups. There's plenty of times where two opposing ideas are presented as ends of a continuum, and that can be confusing if you don't have a clear picture of what you're after. And there are some words (story, plot) that are used in a way that can be non-intuitive, and vagueness in that area is especially damaging given that non-intuitive definition. But outright, game-stopping contradiction? You "can't" do what it says? Nah. You can, plenty did.

      And I haven't forgotten the requests people have for other pieces of the text, I just haven't been at my home computer recently. Before I wrap things up, I will post:

      * The definition/description of a Scene for epweissengruber (BTW, I didn't think your question was derailing, I just didn't understand it at first, I agree having a description of what a session is like is something cool RPGs can have, I haven't found something like that in 1ed. though)

      * See if I can find some exemplar text on Modius, the crappy, weak Prince that's in the sample campaign and sample scenario for Captain Thark.

      If I have forgotten your question-about-the-text or have something you want me to look for, ask now!

      Also, Deliverator, did you see the 2 examples that were examples of when someone failed a non-combat die roll and the GM let it stand? Since I wasn't ever clear on why you were asking about that, I don't know if that's what you were looking for or not?

      \n As to the other stuff: look just at the first quotation, from the intro. JackFractal pointed this out in his first response: It is simply not possible to have "GM decides what happens to PCs" and "life of a character is in player's hands." Those are literally opposites of one another. Sure, sure, you can find some incredibly sophistic way to thread the needle and find a reading of those two sentences to make them reconcile... but in plain-language English, they contradict each other.

      There are other similar issues in some of the other quotations you posted about the jobs of the people playing.

      \n\ncreases \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n edited November 2012
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n There's a principle that scholars and philosophers cite when they're interpreting a text, called the principle of charity. The idea is, when you run into spots where the author says things that, while not being explicitly contradictory on the face of it, run against each other and seem to have contradictory implications and connotations, the reader has to make a choice. Either the author means their words in the plain-language English way, from which it follows that they've contradicted themselves; OR the author isn't contradicting themselves, which means the words don't mean what they do in the plain-language English way. The principle of charity says, go for the interpretation that doesn't impute a contradiction to the author.

      (I'm not totally convinced that there's NO plain-language interpretation of those sentences that makes them consistent, but that's beside the point.)

      If we do that, it doesn't mean the text is above criticism. The author isn't using words in a clear and straightforward way: that's a pretty serious criticism, especially for a text that's supposed to teach something like a game. It also doesn't mean that if you play the game as charitably interpreted, it'll be any fun. We can take @Johnstone at his word, for example, that he played just as @JDCorley is indicating the text says it should be played, and it still just didn't work for his group. All it means is we're trying to be as fair as possible to authors and not attributing straight-up stupid, irrational or incoherent stuff to them.

      I mean, maybe you don't want to do Talmud-like textual exegesis in order to play a game. Nobody should have to! But, you know, the premise of this thread is, let's do a little game scholarship, a little archaeology, right?
      \n
      \n
      \n

Sure, sure, you can find some incredibly sophistic way to thread the needle and find a reading of those two sentences to make them reconcile... but in plain-language English, they contradict each other.
      \nNo they don't, if a player makes decisions and the GM sets consequences for the decisions, those can both be true, in totally normal English.

      It could have been clearer, of course, maybe you shouldn't have had to wait so long to get to that part where it says that's what that means.

      \n I totally agree they are in two different categories. But to say that Monsterhearts, as great as it is, shows how RPG design "generally" is without noticing that there are huge amounts of games that are much more like 1ed. Vampire in their vision of GM as creative director still coming out, and being successful, in larger, consistent numbers seems cockeyed. Monsterhearts is not an example of how RPG design generally is these days.

      (PS Mistborn is also great, better than the novels even.)

      \n I was actually kinda having these same problems in narrowing focus with Changeling the Lost when we played it late last year, and we made extra certain to spend lots of pre-play time making sure we were all on the same page while making characters, and even swore oaths to each others' characters to stay allied and reliable, but in play our interests just started diverging fatally to the point where play was dysfunctional (one or two characters would spend all session advancing their interests while the others tabled theirs, for sessions on end). We postmortemed it a little in this thread, but it got derailed into mostly talking about our mechanical problems rather than our conceptual and logistical ones.

      (oh, and this is a totally different group than the ones I've played oWoD with, I am the only common player or even acquaintance)

      I don't know how fair it is to blame it on CtL (and it's definitely not fair to just put it all on my group either, because we've managed to wrangle functional play out of lots of other games before and since), but I still feel like we got blindsided somehow. It is entirely possible we just don't do well with a game as "open" as the WoD is being praised for being, but we immediately started up an Ars Magica game that is still ongoing and that game is pretty super duper "open" too, but possibly not as inherently/deceptively premiseless. (I think "flee your captors" is just as deceptively inert as "be a vampire") I dunno.

      I definitely have some thoughts to work through regarding lack of default setting vs lack of default (actionable) premise that began with my failures to get a game of Burning Wheel going and are continuing in this thread.

      \n This has been very interesting reading, and I wish I had more to contribute...

      First I guess I'll direct to my post in the "OK, What was VAMPIRE _TO YOU_?" thread, which in a roundabout way tells all I have to tell on the game. I love it to bits. It's been a huge influence on how I play. I think it's a failure from an overall design standpoint. It had a vision, failed to realize it, but still managed to set a direction that was tremendously influential.

      I think its main problem is that it was a very hybrid game. Some of the people who designed it/for it didn't get what it is they were doing/were still entrenched in old ways of designing things. See, for example, the Clans, which in the core book tend to be defined as archetypal subcultures within a micro society, and end up being used as nothing more than "classes" (See the Assamite or Ravnos) in following supplements. Conflicting messages right there. Vampire ceases to be the same game the moment you add a supplement to the core first edition book I feel (would need to go back and re-read the supplements the way I re-read the first edition core book when V20 was released).

      As stated in that other thread, I'm also convinced the game's success is a direct consequences of that design failure.

      JackFractal's comment about "own[ing] a titanic amount of White Wolf material that [he] enjoyed reading, but never played" made me realize how different my own experience happens to be. I don't think I ever bought anything but the core book(s), Chicago by Night and the Giovanni Chronicles. If anything, I'd say the closest recent game to Vampire the way I run it would probably be Apocalypse World (we were just talking about that the other day with one of the people I play with who's currently reading the French edition).
      There's a whole group of players (the one heavily involved in/with the metaplot) I never dabbled with, and only know by hearsay though. Which must color my own vision of the game quite a lot.

      \n I feel there's a lot of things that were overlooked in the supplements, extending into 2ed., yeah.

      Like, what am I exactly supposed to DO with (say) Milwaukee by Night given that the game requires me to select a strong creative direction for my game and get the PCs generated accordingly? If Milwaukee by Night is a sample Chronicle, why is there no strong direction that I provide to the players to help them make PCs and to help me position them appropriately? How does Diablerie: Mexico make any sense at ALL in the context of the main rulebook?

      Other supplements were pretty good about making their purpose clear. The Clanbooks, for all that we kind of poke fun at them today, had a clear purpose - expand on and detail a part of the setting without invalidating what came before. Plus have them be fun to read/appropriate to read for players. Some supplements that had a clear purpose/position were rejected by people - Dirty Secrets of the Black Hand is actually a hell of a lot closer to a "Chronicle book" than Chicago by Night is. There is a clear initial situation, character generation information, direction and tone, all that stuff. And late in the Revised cycle there were some VERY good supplements like the Archons & Templars book and Gilded Cage, which was all about Influences.

      \n
      Most of your arguments rest on "I've never seen it happen so I doubt it's important" and "This game sold lots of copies so it must have been doing something right", which are fine opinions but really terrible arguments. For the first, I've seen it happen and seen it plenty so I can't believe it's NOT important, and if sales are inherent proof of merit or artistic success then I guess you've never, ever seen something super popular that you completely abhor?

      This is a dead line of argument that relies on marginalizing opposing opinions, based only on your own. I'm certainly not denying your right to stick up for Vampire, nor am I trying to pretend you didn't have fun with the game or other people did, but just saying so doesn't settle the argument for anyone who disagrees. I could say "Large numbers of Vampire were bought by people who read the books instead of playing them, or to people who played a couple games then quit", with just as much evidence as you've given.

      (please don't start an argument over that, I'm throwing it out as an example of a BAD argument with no way to prove it that only annoys people whose experience contradicts it)
      \nI see your point, Joe, seriously. And I said that I had an evidence as anecdotical as yours.

      Definitely, there are very popular things I hate. For example, I don't really consider the Call of Duty videogames true games, but more like movies in which you mash some buttons to move from scene to scene and where you never ever make any kind of meaningful decision. But I wouldn't go as far as call that design terribad, as many many persons buy, play and enjoy those games. I admit that there are design philosophies that do not work for me, and go on my merry way not playing those games and playing others I like more. But calling those games invalid is dishonest, to me.

      And I would not call them "toxic" for the social group of gamers, as I have never met any person that has been turned into a toxic one by any entertainment product, and in 10 years of psychotherapy practice I've seen really toxic people. Vampire was not toxic for anyone that wasn't already toxic himself: a game does not turn you into a dick if you are not already one. The system mechanics of Vampire have plenty of things to criticize, but I don't see how "You are a vampire" is a less valid premise than "you are a Mormon gunman that wanders around smiting evil without any fucking guidance to what evil is other than your opinion." A group of dicks playing DitV by the book will have a shitty game, for sure. I've seen it happen.

      The problem with many of these anti-Vampire / CoC / D&D / other trad games positions is that system matters, certainly, but systems don't change people, and you cannot fix a group by playing a very defined game. Also, I find really weird that fixation to the literal text of the book, like any interpretation of the same is a fucking heresy or it necessarily ends in tears. I mean, Sorcerer can be so fucking confusing that this Kubasik guy was writing a book clarifying how the fuck it should be played. There were a bajillion threads in the Forge asking for clarifications about things like who the fuck gets to act first in a combat. Many people gets stumped with combat in BW. And so and so and so. And these are completely regular RPGs by any account, with some very good GMing advice.

      I understand that a game that tries to cover such a wide range of premises may be confusing, hard, or just fail for some people. But the argument that "despite it selling lots of books, we do not know how many of those books were used in actual play" does not hold any water. There are communities many times the size of this of people who actively play the game. There are cons. There are LARP events by the THOUSANDS.

      All those incoherent games that are allegedly so difficult to get and need so many fixes keep on print for decades, have a strong presence in many gaming events, are discussed, run and played everywhere. Of course, a part of the sales will be due to inactive gamers that only read them, but no one can think that those are a majority by any stretch. And I agree that most games, trad or not, can always improve a bit their playing and GMing advice.

      I guess that I take exception at the very extremist and, from my POV, completely unfounded positions held by some theorists that some very famous games were unplayable as written and if someone was having fun with them was due to massive drifting (and even if that was true, someone should demonstrate that drifting is necessarily bad) or because they deceived themselves. But again, I read things like Playing with Intent and I wonder which kind of shitty groups you need to meet to feel that you have to take that many defensive measures.

      I understand that a game that tries to cover such a wide range of premises may be confusing, hard, or just fail for some people.       \nAnd we do not know how many other THOUSANDS may or may not have tried Vampire and given up. WE DO NOT KNOW, and you seem to be more than willing to discount the individual reports of people who had just that experience, apparently because you have not sat at their tables. And that is what annoys me about the "Vampire is super functional, just look at all these dudes playing it" defenses by dudes that apparently have not seen it blow up in their faces despite best intents, as I have. You look at functional play and think nonfunctional is abnormal and rare, I look at nonfunctional, and the posts of other people who feel similarly, and think maybe this game is deceptively tricky to work with for certain kinds of people and really poor about indicating it.

      I am not one of those guys who says "Vampire is a cancer on the hobby", or whatever, and my posts you quoted to respond to do not use "toxic" or any of the other hot-buttons you bring up in your response. I don't think anyone in this thread has except the VtM defense team accusing other people of doing it!

      All I'm saying is, when you are actually talking to a guy who says "I tried Vampire and couldn't get it to work", just saying "all these other people somehow managed (so why can't you)" and dropping the mic is not helpful. Yeah, Kubasik started to write Play Sorcerer. You would rather he just scoffed at the people who weren't having fun, like I rather feel you are doing, instead of trying to demystify the process he used to find the fun?

      \n JD, this thread is great, especially your back-and-forth with Eero. I certainly did this when I was 18, but I think it would be different stuff in both cases now.

      \n I was surprised by the pervasiveness of the "become human again" potential Chronicle in 1ed. I remembered it was there, but probably there isn't a chapter that doesn't include it, and it's in the friggin' art!

      Here's the definition of "Scene":\n
      \u2022 Scene - One compact period of time in one location. It is made up of a variable number of Tums (as many as it takes to complete it) or is straightforward roleplaying.
      \n"As many as it takes to complete it" (wah wah trumpet sound plays)

      Here's another:\n
      \u2022 Scene: A single episode of the story; a time and place where actions and events take place moment by moment. A Scene is often a dramatic high-point of the story.
      \n...often?!?! How often exactly?!?

      And here's the broader explanation from later on in the book:\n
      A Scene is a moment in a story when you focus on the events happening at hand, and roleplay them through as if they were really occurring. This may involve only roleplaying and a lot of conversation between the players and the Storyteller (who are all "in character" ), but may also involve a number of different actions, some of them requiring the rolling of dice. If it is more game than roleplaying, then it is called an Action Scene (which is discussed below).

      A Scene is like a series of shots taken in a movie in the same location and at the same moment in the story. It is the real gist of the roleplaying, the time when the players are actively in their roles and are able to describe their characters' reactions to events rather than explanations of things they intend to do.
      \nThat actually makes a bit more sense, it contrasts to non-roleplayed downtime, which is also described.

      Still, contradiction found, and actually a pretty serious one since some Disciplines have Scene-length durations. WHOOPS

      And finally, the Inspiration list:\n
      Novels: The Vampire Chronicles, Anne Rice (read all three books), Dracula, Bram Stoker, Those Who Haunt The Night, Barbara Hambly, Sandman (a graphic novel), Time Enough For Love, Robert Heinlein
      \nYes, they did not list Neil Gaiman as the author of Sandman, "(a graphic novel)". "read all three books", by the way.\n
      Movies: Near Dark, Vamp, The Hunger, Nosferatu (the original silent movie), Dracula (the original talkie), Blue Velvet, Rear Window, Lost Boys, Alien, Aliens
      \nI think it's telling that Near Dark is listed first.\n
      Games: Ars Magica, Runequest, Shadowrun, Call of Cthulhu, Pendragon, Gurps Horror, Corps, Illuminati, Dungeons and Dragons

      Other: Everything written by Carl Jung, Joseph Campbell, Milan Kundera, Herman Hesse, Albert Camus, P.B. Shelley, Mercea Eliade, Ayn Rand & (of course) Vaclav Havel
      \nAyn Rand, and OF COURSE Vaclav Havel.

      LOLlin big time over here.

      \nRight.

      So yeah, that and the pervasive "becoming human" chronicle (well, I guess Anne Rice had something to do with it too) is what cemented Vampire as primarily Emo Gothic Torment in my mind (which I don't mean in a bad way!). That was totally my "in", which is why I was always kinda culture shocked whenever I met someone who was all "no way, it's totally about No Exit backbiting forever". I guess most of the By Night books and modules catered to that impression, but, uh, frankly there was so much stuff in those that struck me as the Bad Wrong Fun that I kind of wrote them off even at the time.

      \n
      I was surprised by the pervasiveness of the "become human again" potential Chronicle in 1ed. I remembered it was there, but probably there isn't a chapter that doesn't include it, and it's in the friggin' art!
      \nI remember thinking, upon first reading the pitch of Dead Inside, "Well, seems like someone tried to tackle one of the games Vampire wanted to be at last"... If anything that was always my one of my main draws to the game, and I sure as hell made more Beast checks to see how one would behave with a family member than for firefights (though rest assured we had some of those, sometimes so silly and nonsensical I can't but smile just at the thought)...

      To me, Vampire was at the intersection of political and personal drama. The characters were pretty much immortal, so the game's stakes had to be about what can be worse than dying - the added superpowers were just a way to mitigate how mean the game had to be to work, an empowerment to compensate for how bleak the game could be otherwise. As the notes to my personal set of rules state in opening: it was a game about alienation and the dissolution of the self. On that front, from the get go, I didn't like the humanity sub system, or the fact that Experience was somehow going at cross purpose with it.

      At least that's the angle I always worked from first edition onward, ignoring most of the stuff later added by supplements as much as I could get away with - though again, don't take me too seriously, we did end up with a firefight of four people against a giant snake crammed into an armored Renault 5... and damn was it lovely.

      \n\nLisa Padol \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2012 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n So... when have I actually played Vampire: The Masquerade?

      1. A friend's home campaign where the PCs were secret heroes. I was told "Take 6th generation, and then take 500 freebie points". Lots of fun, but not a lot of internal stuff. I can live with that.

      2. This DexCon, Jason Schneiderman (I think) ran a game that, at its purest, could have been a larp with premise: Ten vampires. One serial killer who has to be stopped wrecking things for everyone. Ten different desires about what to do with the killer.

      In play, it was a tabletop, and we had three players, so the other PC roles were NPCs, as the PCs were all connected to each other. But, deciding what to do once the killer was stopped was probably easier than it would have been if all the roles had been filled.

      I ran it once, and was surprised when the players didn't see the plot twist coming (I was running the first scenario released for the game). This taught me the obvious: The players haven't read the scenario. What is obvious to you will be completely opaque to them. They're not being stupid -- you have the full story. They don't. Keep remembering this.

      That's generally good advice for mysteries -- and I do run a lot of Call of Cthulhu plots. For mystery or intrigue, make sure you can keep track of what's going on and what the players do and don't know. If you want them to understand something, make sure they have the correct information. Indeed, err on the side of disclosure, I think, especially for Vampire intrigue, as it really sucks to have one's PC fucked over because the GM thought you could read her mind.

      \n Hey everyone, coming back to this thread to mention I did finally get a PDF of Chicago by Night 1st Edition (1991...although there is no copyright year in this book!) and just as I remembered, there is only about half a page of "how to use this book", it suggests you adapt the Theme of your campaign to the Chicago by Night's Theme (and not the other way around, which would be useful) and contains exactly ZERO information on situating the player characters into the game material through either the pre-generated CbN1 Theme or your own CbN1-adjusted Theme.

      So I think critiques that "Vampire doesn't tell you how to play" are much more valid when it comes to supplemental material, and very early on, too.

      Interestingly, CbN1 also includes maybe the first relationship chart that I remember seeing printed in a RPG book, though it's for reference only and there are no mechanics associated with it.

      I had completely forgotten about the "Encounters" section, which, if expanded, could have been the building block for a stunning nonlinear supplement. You could either roll 2d10 or select from a list of different encounters for different themes/subjects/feelings. It's pretty great.

      Here's an example encounter from the "Intrigue" chart: A street gang tries rather obviously to pick a fight with the characters. If the characters don't back down, a kindred hiding among them tries to recruit them for allies in an upcoming gang war.

      Here's one from the "Nostalgia" chart: You suddenly smell, extremely vividly, a smell that takes you back to your mortal life somehow. You are lost in a memory that is dear to you. The traffic light changes and everyone's honking their horns at you and screaming.

      It's awesome. I think for what Vampire 1ed. was pushing, a whole book of these things with different themes/tones would have been terrific.

      (There are no page numbers on the table of contents.

      But there is an index, LOL.)

      \n My favorite "Conspiracy" encounter: a young boy bumps into the character at a convenience store. He sizes up the character and then offers to tell the character a secret. If you squoosh Mounds bars flat you can fit them through the broken ventilation fan in the back of the store. Only three other people in the world know this. You must tell no one. Outside the store the boy and two others, eating Mounds bars, give you a thumbs up through the window.

      ^.^ I'm in a conspiracy!!!

      (But this actually does keep the idea of conspiracy on everyone's minds so it's kind of genius.)

      (Also another example of how Vampire was not a 100% Serious Dramatic Game With Important Values Expressed Through Play)
